The asphalt does not remember the cannery it replaced, nor the field before the cannery, nor the low houses before the field, nor the tannery smoke that hung over them, nor the family that hauled its water across that dirt and argued and laughed and buried its dead in a language the lot has long since paved over. But the oak remembers in the only way a tree remembers, which is to go on standing on the spot; and the hawk remembers in the only way her kind remembers, which is to come back, and come back, to the same branch over the same ground across more lifetimes than the people below have ever had names for.

She holds still on the branch, in the gold eye, in that long patience that does not trouble to tell a century from an afternoon; and the asphalt thins, and the fence with it, and the cannery and the field thin away behind them; and the smoke comes up again, low, over a close poor cluster of houses; and the morning is another morning, earlier and harder and still entirely ahead of itself; and down in the swept dirt of the yard a child too small for the bucket riding his hip stops, and feels a shadow cross him, and tips his face up to find it.

Nothing, yet, has been lost.

### Chapter — El Halcón

A hundred-some years before that bright loud summer morning — before the asphalt, before the Dipper, before the surfer in the barrel — the same warm wind worked the same ridge, and a hawk rode it down toward the same bay: this one's great-great-grandmother, or near enough, hunting the same ravine, over a country not yet smudged at the river's mouth by more than a single thin column of tannery smoke. She came down the falling land the way her line always had — off the redwoods that were old when the Mission was not yet a thought, down past the madrone and the manzanita, along the San Lorenzo where the steelhead held against the current, out over the gold meadows and the dunes to the gray breathing edge of the Pacific — and then banked inland again on a held wing, unhurried, and dropped the last of the height, and set her wings, and took a high bare limb of a live oak on the edge of a poor cluster of houses in the tannery's shadow, where the smell of the hides was, where the river met the work. She folded her wings. She fixed the round gold eye on the dirt yard below.

And below, a small brown boy, four years old, hauling a bucket he is too small to haul, stops, and feels the shadow pass over him, and looks up — the way he will look up his whole life, the way that will frighten his grandfather and the country both — and sees her there against the white sky, enormous and still, and does not run, and is not afraid, and lifts one hand to her as if to someone he already knows.

His grandfather, watching from the doorway, sees the boy and the bird regard each other, and something very old turns over in the old man's chest. *El halcón,* Don Aurelio says under his breath, half a blessing and half a fear. *Mira, mijo. The hawk has come to look at you.* And from that morning it was the boy's name in the family — El Halcón, the hawk — the name his own people gave him out of love and the old knowledge, long before the Eastern papers would take one look at a brown man who could throw and hand him instead a dead Spanish conqueror's name, *El Cid,* and call it a compliment. He carried two names his whole life and answered, in his heart, only to the first: the one the hawk gave him, that meant *he belongs to the high air and the deep country and the people who watch the sky;* the one the women softened, when they wanted him near, to the smallest and the largest word there is.

*Mijo,* his mother called from the house. *Mijo — leave the bird and bring the water.*

### Chapter — The Mission

Before the Méndez had anything to lose, somebody else had already lost it to them; and Don Aurelio, who was an honest man in the particular way only the very old can afford to be, never once told the family's story without telling that part too — though it would have been easier, and more flattering, to leave it out.

Long before the Americans, before even the grant — before there was a Villa de Branciforte or a single adobe on the river — there were the people the Spanish called the Costanoans, the coast people, who had lived along that bay for longer than anyone could count: who had eaten from it and burned the meadows to tend it and buried their dead on the same hills the Méndez would one day climb to bury theirs. They had a word for themselves that was not Costanoan, that the Spanish never troubled to learn. And the Spanish came up the coast with the cross in one hand and the brand in the other, and built a mission — Santa Cruz, the Holy Cross — and gathered the coast people into it to save their souls and break their bodies, and called them neophytes, and worked them; and the measles and the smallpox and the simple crushing grief did the rest, so that within two generations the people who had owned that bay since before Rome were a remnant, a handful, a name in a baptismal register kept by a priest in a language not their own. And when Mexico threw off Spain and broke up the missions and handed the land out in great grants to its loyal men, the Méndez were among the men it was handed *to* — and the land it was handed *from* was the land of the coast people, who were given nothing, who were not even in the room, who were by then so far under the wheel that no one thought to count them as having been robbed at all.

*Remember that, mijo,* the old man would say, and his voice when he said it carried no self-pity and no self-excuse either, only the flat terrible honesty of a man reading the whole ledger. *When the Americans took our rancho, we cried that it was stolen, and we were right. But we were standing on it. We did not buy that land from anyone who had the right to sell it — the King of Spain gave it, and the King of Spain had it because his soldiers took it from people who are mostly in the ground now, with no grandsons left to climb a hill and pull the weeds and say their name. So when you feel the curse on us, the always-second, the being-pushed-down — do not let it make you innocent. We were pushed down by men who were once us. The wheel does not turn only against the Méndez. It turns. It has always only turned. And the only question a man gets to answer is not whether he was wronged — everyone was wronged, everyone did wrong, the whole coast is built on bones — but whether, when it is briefly his turn near the top of the wheel, he reaches down or he reaches up.* And then the old man would fall quiet a while, and look toward the hills where both the coast people and the Méndez children lay in their separate, unequal grounds, and say the thing that was the deepest root of everything he taught the boy: *We are not cursed because we are good and they are bad. There is no such story, not really. We are cursed the way everyone is cursed — by being part of it. The grace is not in being clean. Nobody is clean. The grace is in what you do with dirty hands.*

### Chapter — The Burn

The coast people set the world on fire every year, on purpose, with love — and it was the most careful tending that ground had ever known, or would know again until long after everyone who remembered how to do it was dead.

They burned the meadows in the dry season, low cool fires walked across the land in the evening when the wind lay down; and the fire was not destruction to them but its opposite, the central act of keeping. It cleared the brush before the brush could choke the oaks; it brought the deer to the tender green that rose after; it kept the meadows open and the seed-grasses generous; it gave the willow and the hazel the straight new shoots a woman needed to weave a basket tight enough to hold water. The land they had was not wilderness. That was the lie the newcomers told themselves to feel clean about taking it — that it was empty, untended, *wild,* a thing no one had made. It was made. It was the most made thing on the whole coast, made by ten thousand years of small wise fires, a garden the size of a country, tended so lightly that the conquerors could not even see it was a garden, and called it wilderness, and improved it into ruin.

For the Spanish, and the Americans after them, fire was the devil's own tool, and they forbade the burning — and the land, untended, did what untended land does: it thickened, and choked, and grew rank with the very fuel the small fires had always eaten, the brush piling up dry under the trees year on year, an account quietly building toward a reckoning. The careful annual grief of the cool walking fires was outlawed as savagery, in the name of order; and the order was a lie, because what it actually grew was not safety but the slow patient loading of a gun. Don Aurelio, who had seen the meadows in the last years anyone burned them and watched the hills grow sick and shaggy and dangerous in the decades after, understood the thing the coast people had known and the newcomers could not afford to learn — the same thing Dolores knew about grief, that she gave its Sunday hour and not one inch more: that there is a kind of fire that is tending, the small regular honest losses a living thing must take to stay alive; and that a people, or a family, or a land that forbids itself those small fires in the name of never letting anything burn is only saving it all up. The fire still comes. It always comes. And when it has been forbidden long enough, it comes all at once, and takes everything, and is called a tragedy — as though no one had spent forty tidy years building it.

### Chapter — The Grant

Before this was a town for strangers — before the lights on the water and the laughing on the pier — it was ours. All of it. Don Aurelio would say it the way other men say prayers, which is to say he said it until the words wore smooth and stopped meaning anything to anyone but himself; and yet it was true, and the truth of it was the whole foundation the family stood on, long after the family had nothing else left to stand on at all.

In 1833 the young Mexican republic — hungry, idealistic, and broke — did a thing both generous and ruinous: it unlocked the missions, broke the friars' long hold on the coast, and handed God's land back to men. A governor's agent rode down from Monterey with a leather folio of grants, and one of them, when the dust settled, made Don Joaquín Méndez — Cirilo's great-great-grandfather, a blunt, devout, illiterate man who signed with a cross and wept doing it — a *ranchero.* The grant ran from the river to the redwood line, some leagues of hills the color of a lion in August, and Don Joaquín stood at the edge of it the first evening and could not speak, because no man in his family, as far back as the family could count, had ever owned anything a king could not take back on a whim.

They raised the low white adobe that held the cool like a cellar, and a chapel beside it, and ran the first cattle down into the bottoms, and for one brief generation the valley belonged, more or less, to the people who worked it. *Gente de razón,* they called themselves — people of reason, people of name — Mexicans and Catholics and, before and above all of it, owners.

But the hands that built the adobe and worked the cattle were not, most of them, Méndez hands, and this is the part of the story Don Aurelio told less often, and more quietly, and only when he was being honest — which old age made him more, and not less. The vaqueros and the diggers and the women at the Méndez fires were Ohlone, Awaswas people, the ones who had been at the mission before there was a Méndez grant to work, who came out of the broken mission with their old world gone and their new one already owned by men like Don Joaquín. The finest of the Méndez riders was one of them — a lean, silent man the family called Esteban, the *first* Esteban, the one Cirilo's father would be named for three generations on — who could gentle a wild horse with his breath and his hands, and who had been born, the mission books said, in a village whose name no one living could any longer pronounce. He worked the land the Méndez had been given. He owned none of it. The family that would spend the next hundred years being dispossessed had been handed its one brief kingdom, in part, on the back of a man more dispossessed than they would ever be.

It is a particular thing, to be born into a family that owned the ground beneath its own feet, and then to watch that ownership carried off across the length of a single life. It teaches you a lesson the secure never have to learn — that everything is a loan, and that the lenders, when they come to collect, do not knock. But there is a harder lesson underneath it, the one Don Aurelio reached only at the very end: that his own people had, for one generation, been lenders too; that the wheel which ground them had ground others first beneath their own weight; and that the only hands in the whole long story that were ever truly clean were the hands of the people who had never been given anything to dirty them with.

### Chapter — California Banknotes

The wealth came on the hoof and left on the hide, and the whole bright machinery of it ran on blood and salt and the patience of men who owned nothing.

Each spring the vaqueros rode the cattle down out of the hills for the *matanza* — the great killing — and for a week the bottoms ran red and the gulls came inland in white storms and the work went on from dark to dark. The Ohlone riders did the killing and the Méndez men kept the count, and the women boiled the tallow in iron pots big enough to bathe a child in, and the hides went out to dry, staked in their hundreds to the earth like a field of dark windows turned up at the sky. It was filthy, holy, exhausting work, and the smell of it — tallow smoke and blood and salt and green hide — was the smell of money, exactly the way the tannery's stink would be the smell of money to the same family eighty years on, when the hides belonged to a Prussian and the family only worked them.

For in those years a cowhide was as good as coin, and better. The Yankee ships came round the Horn and stood off the cove, and their captains — lean, courteous, devouring men, the polite leading edge of an empire that had not yet bothered to announce itself — traded silver and silk and Boston boots and bolts of cloth for the *cueros* the rancheros rowed out to them. *California banknotes,* the traders called the hides, and laughed when they said it, and meant it, and wrote down in their ledgers, in a language no ranchero could read, the exact future price of everything. One of them, a Captain Bray out of Boston who came three seasons running, ate at the Méndez table and admired the chapel and learned to dance the jota badly and with great good will, and who would live long enough to file, in 1852, one of the squatter claims that helped take the grant apart. He bore the family no ill will at all. That was the terrible thing about the men who took it: so few of them bore any ill will. They wanted the land the way water wants the downhill, and they came, and they were charming, and they stayed for supper — and then one morning the supper was theirs.

And on the rancho, in a shed that smelled of oak bark and lime, the family tanned its own leather, from its own herds, on its own ground. Hold on to that through everything that follows: the leather was theirs before it was anyone's. The hands knew the work because the work had once been a kind of kingship.

Across the San Lorenzo, the rough little pueblo of Branciforte gambled and brawled and drank — a town the Spanish crown had stocked, a lifetime before, with the swept-out contents of a Guadalajara jail, petty criminals for whom a stretch of empty California coast had been a sentence and a mercy at once — and on feast nights the guitars from the Méndez fandango carried over the black water to meet the fiddles and the cursing of the pueblo, so that the sacred bank of the river and the profane bank of the river sang to each other across the dark, and a boy lying awake in the white adobe could believe the whole arrangement of the world was permanent, and benevolent, and his.

It seemed it would last forever. It lasted about as long as one strong man's memory. Nothing built on land ever lasts longer than that — the land outlives everyone, and keeps no one's name, and that is the first lesson it teaches and the last, and almost no one learns it until the deed is already in another man's hand.

### Chapter — The Otters

Before they took the land, they took the otters; and the otters were the first lesson, the one nobody learned, in what that coast was going to be *for.*

The sea otter wears the densest fur in the animal world — near a million hairs to the square inch, for it has no blubber and must hold the cold sea off with pelt alone — and that fur, in the markets of Canton, was worth a small fortune, and so the otter was the first wealth of that coast to be noticed by the wider hungry world. Long before the hide ships, before the gold, before a single rancho was surveyed into squares, the fur hunters came — Russians and the Aleut men they pressed into the killing, Yankee and Spanish and English captains — and worked the kelp beds and the rocky points and the river mouths, and took the otters; took them by the thousand and the ten thousand; took them, with the terrible efficiency of a market that feels no cost it is not charged for, very nearly all the way down to none. Within a few decades the sea that had swarmed with them lay nearly empty of them, and the trade, having exhausted the thing it fed on, simply moved along the coast to the next bay and the next, the way such trades do, leaving silence behind in the kelp.

And the kelp itself paid for it, in the second lesson nobody learned: for the otters had eaten the urchins, and with the otters gone the urchins multiplied unchecked and mowed the great kelp forests down to bare rock, urchin barrens where a drowned green country had swayed — so that the kelp the hawk saw rocking offshore in Cirilo's boyhood was already a remnant of a remnant, the whole near-shore world quietly poorer, its richest weave unraveled, because of a fashion in a city on the far side of an ocean that would never see the cove it had emptied. This was the pattern, set on that coast before any human living there was dispossessed: find the most valuable thing, take all of it, take it for a distant market that is charged nothing for the damage, and move on. The otters were only the rehearsal. The hides were next, and then the redwoods, and then the lime, and then — for it is the same act exactly, only the cargo changes — the men in the kilns and the fields and the tannery, spent and taken and replaced, for markets that would never see the canyon or the culvert or the cost.

A few otters lived. A remnant hid in one cove down the wild coast and was overlooked, and held on, and a century on began, slowly, to come back along the shore — the engine again, the old absolute refusal to be entirely finished off; like the basketweaver on the wharf, like the family on the hill, not erased after all but only reduced, only waiting, only second for a while to the force that had emptied the sea, and patient, and not yet done.

### Chapter — The Fandango

There was one night Don Aurelio returned to all his life, though he had not been born in time for it; he had it from his father, who had it as a boy, and he told it so often and so well that it became, in the family, less a memory than a kind of scripture — the night of the great fandango, the high-water mark of everything, the last good year before any of them knew there had ever been a last good year.

It was the close of the *matanza,* the spring the hides ran to nine hundred, and Don Joaquín — the patriarch who had wept signing the grant — threw the whole rancho open. They roasted a steer and two pigs over pits dug that afternoon; they hung lanterns in the oaks; the women had been three days at the tamales and the women's mothers three days at the women. By dark the yard was full: Méndez cousins down from Branciforte, rancheros from three valleys, the vaqueros in their best with silver on their hatbands earned across a hard year — and the Ohlone families who worked the place gathered at the edge of the light, where the custom of the country kept them, near enough to the music to dance to it and far enough from the fire to remember whose fire it was.

A guitar started, and then another, and then old blind Cipriano's fiddle, and the jota went up into the oaks and the yard began to turn. And the finest dancer of the whole night — every account agreed, even the ones that wished otherwise — was the Ohlone vaquero the family called Esteban, the first Esteban, the one Cirilo's father would be named for three generations on: a man who could gentle a horse with his breath and who danced the way he rode, all stillness and sudden grace, his boots going like a drum on the packed earth while the rest of him stayed quiet as a held note. The Yankee was there too — Captain Bray, off the hide ship, three seasons a guest, learning the jota badly and laughing at himself and being loved for the laughing, a courteous, devouring man who admired the chapel and the dancing and the daughters, and who was, though no soul in that firelight could have known it, the polite advance scout of the very thing that would carry all of this off inside ten years. He danced with Don Joaquín's nieces. He toasted the old man's health in bad Spanish. And he noted, meaning nothing by it yet, the run of the creek and the line of the hills.

And a boy of nine — the boy who would grow into Don Aurelio's father, who would believe the treaty and lose the rancho and end with a measure of his mind gone — sat on the chapel step for half the night and watched it all: the turning yard, the leaping fire, his grandfather presiding like a king over a country that had not yet been told it was about to stop being a country. He carried that night intact through everything that came after, the way a man carries the one photograph he saved from a fire, and he handed it down to his son Aurelio, who handed it down the line — so that eighty years later, on a tannery floor and a Brooklyn ball field and a Boston deathbed, some buried Méndez cell still remembered, without knowing that it remembered, the smell of tallow smoke and crushed sage and the sound of a blind man's fiddle going up into the oaks on the last good night of the world that was theirs. It seemed it would last forever. It is always, in every life and every country, the nights that seem they will last forever that turn out, looked back upon, to have been the last.

### Chapter — The Courthouse

The conquest, when it came, did not come with a sword. It came with a courthouse, which is worse, because a sword at least admits what it is.

The thunder came from the north first, in 1846: a flag run up by a mob of American settlers at Sonoma, with a bear on it that looked, the Californios said, like a badly drawn pig; then a war that was over almost before the rancho had heard it begun; then the stars and stripes raised over the customhouse at Monterey on a July morning where the eagle of Mexico had flown the day before. Don Joaquín was an old man by then. He rode up to see it, and came home and said nothing for three days, and then said only: *They have planted their flag. Now we will see whether they keep their word.*

In 1848 came the word, on paper, signed and sworn: the Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo, by which the United States, upon its honor, guaranteed to the conquered Californios the full enjoyment of their property — that what was theirs would remain theirs. Don Joaquín's son, Don Aurelio's own father, a gentler and more trusting man than the old ranchero, believed it entirely. That was his single crime, and it cost him the rancho, his health, and at the last a measure of his mind.

For the new country did not take the land at gunpoint. It took it by *process,* which is slower, and leaves no scar a man can point to. The Land Act of 1851 required every Californio to appear before a commission — seated in San Francisco, three hard days to the north — and prove, in English, with documents, before American lawyers, that the ground beneath his own chapel was lawfully his; and the burden of proof lay on the man who already held the land. Don Aurelio went north with his father, a boy of fourteen carrying a leather folio of Mexican grants and *diseños* — hand-drawn maps with a sycamore and a creek-bend and a cattle skull for boundary markers — into a paneled room where a commissioner named Hollister, a decent and overworked man who privately thought the whole business a disgrace and approved the claim in the end, took four years to do it. Four years of an attorney named Quint, of San Francisco, who took his fee in land because the family had no cash: a quarter-league for a filing, a quarter-league for an appeal, so that the family won its case and lost its rancho in the same long breath, paying the men who saved the title with the very acres the title covered. Quint was not a wicked man. He had a wife and a pew and a reputation for fairness. He simply understood, as the rancheros did not, that in the new country the land itself was no longer the wealth — the *paper* was the wealth; and he dealt in paper, and the Californios came to him holding deeds in a dead king's hand and walked out holding nothing.

And then, as though the courthouse had not been enough, the sky turned. The great flood of the early sixties drowned the valley bottoms and the herds with them, and behind the flood came the long drought of '64, two years without rain, the hills gone white with the bones of cattle that had been California banknotes and were now not worth the cost of skinning. The cattle economy that had built the rancho died in a single dry season, up and down the whole coast, and the Méndez sold what the lawyers had left them the way a man bleeds — not all at once, but a parcel for a debt and a parcel for a winter, until there was no more to sell.

It was somewhere in those years that Don Aurelio, a young man watching his father fail, began to believe the thing he would carry the rest of his life and never quite say aloud: that the family was *cursed* now — cursed to second, that the conquest had taken not only the land but the right ever again to be first at anything — and that the world would collect, hard and without mercy and on its own schedule, from any Méndez fool enough to forget it. He was wrong. But he had earned the error honestly, in a paneled room in San Francisco, watching a decent man named Hollister take four years to hand his family back a kingdom that a decent man named Quint had already quietly carried off in his briefcase.

### Chapter — The Hearing

And here is the other night the family carried, the black twin of the fandango: the day in San Francisco, ten years on, when they began to lose all of it in a paneled room.

Don Aurelio was fourteen, and he had come north with his father on the coastal steamer — three days of seasickness and dread — carrying a leather folio that held the whole of the family's claim to the earth it had lived on for twenty years: the Mexican grant with the governor's seal, and the *diseño,* the map, drawn in his grandfather's own hand, a thing of beauty and uselessness, with a sycamore for one corner and the bend of the creek for another and a cattle skull, carefully inked, to mark where the land met the sea. In the country that had made that map, every man who mattered knew the sycamore and the creek-bend and the skull. In the country that had replaced it, the map was a child's drawing, and the room they carried it into did not run on knowing the land. It ran on paper, and on English, and on time — of which it had an infinite supply and the Méndez had none.

The commissioner was a man named Hollister, gray and overworked and — Aurelio decided, across the four years it took — not a bad man: one who privately thought the whole proceeding a national disgrace, and who would in the end rule for the family; but who had four hundred such claims stacked on his docket and one ordinary mortal lifespan in which to hear them, and who therefore let each grind forward at the speed of cold honey, asking through an interpreter the same question a fourth and a fifth time, requiring a boundary witness who had to be fetched three hundred miles, adjourning, adjourning, adjourning. And the family's own attorney — a courtly San Franciscan named Quint, who took the case because there was a fee in it, and the fee, since the Méndez had no cash, was *land* — sat at the father's elbow through all of it being, by the standards of his profession, scrupulously fair: a quarter-league deeded over for the filing, a quarter-league for the first appeal, a quarter-league when the rival claim (entered, as it happened, by a genial retired sea-captain named Bray, who bore the family no ill will at all) had to be answered.

Aurelio sat on a hard bench against the wall and watched his father — who had believed the treaty — come slowly to understand, across four years, that he had been robbed without anyone in the room ever once doing a thing a sheriff could arrest them for; that the robbery *was* the process, not a break in it; that he would win, and be ruined by the winning, and that there was no man he could call out, no door he could kick, no single villain anywhere in the whole patient machine. It was the having-no-one-to-hate, Don Aurelio would say eighty years later, that broke his father's mind. A man can survive being robbed. It is being robbed *politely* — lawfully, by decent men following the rules, with a receipt — that there is no surviving.

They rode home down the coast with the title confirmed and the rancho three-quarters gone, and Don Joaquín met them at the landing, very old now, and read his son's face before a word was spoken, and only nodded — the way you nod at news you have been expecting for years. *They kept their word,* the old man said at last, looking out at the hills that were no longer theirs. *That was the cruelest thing they could have done. If they had only lied to us, we would at least have had something left to forgive.* And he turned and walked up toward the chapel, and that was the last anyone remembered him saying about it, ever.

### Chapter — The Culvert

What Esteban Méndez saw from the culvert when he was twelve years old, he never told his children, and they never asked — because a man's children learn early which doors in him do not open; but they lived their whole lives inside the house that day built, and so, in a way, they knew it without being told.

It was in the bad years, after the land was gone, when the family had fallen from the rancho to wage-work and the county had filled up with new men who did not know the Méndez had ever been anyone and would not have cared — years when a Mexican in that part of California was a thing it was safe to do something to, because the law that had taken the land had also, quietly, stopped counting Mexican grief as the kind that summoned consequences. Esteban had been fast then — the quickest boy in the flats, faster than any Anglo kid in the county — and a boy that fast draws a crowd, and a crowd draws the wrong kind of attention; and the attention, that day, did not fall on Esteban. It fell, the way it so often fell, on the one standing nearest him who was darker still: his friend Toussaint, a Black boy who could hit a ball into the next county, who answered for a crime that was really only the crime of having been admired alongside a fast boy the county had not yet decided how to fear — and answered for it with his life, off a low oak limb. And young Esteban, shoved down and held there by bigger hands that had the sense he did not yet have, had gone still as the stone in the mouth of a culvert under the road.

And from there, through a screen of weeds, in a culvert that smelled of mud and cold iron, twelve years old, he watched. He watched the whole of it and made no sound — because making a sound would have meant two of them in the road instead of one, and some animal arithmetic in him had already done that sum; and he was right, and it saved his life, and it cost him the rest of his life, both, in the same held breath. When the horses had gone he did not come out for a long time. When he came out he was the man he would be forever after: the gray careful man, the man who flinched at raised voices and loud joy alike, the man who wanted nothing from the world except to be beneath its notice, who taught his sons by example that the safest size for a Méndez to be was small, the safest sound none, the safest dream no dream at all. People who did not know took it for weakness, even cowardice. It was neither. It was a wound — perfectly rational, dressed every morning and never healed: a man who had learned at twelve, definitively, in a culvert, what the world did to those who stood up in the road — and to those who only stood beside them when they did, for it was his own quickness that had drawn the eyes that killed his friend — and who loved his children too much to want them ever to learn it the way he had. And the terrible thing — the thing Cirilo understood only as a grown man, with his own son coming, the thing that broke his heart finally and completely about his father — was that Esteban was not wrong. He was, in the plain bookkeeping of that country in those years, simply correct. To reach was to be aimed at. He had the evidence in his own eyes, screened by weeds, for the whole of his silent life. And he had spent that life trying to give his sons the one gift the culvert had given him, which he believed with his whole frightened loving heart was the only gift that would keep them alive: the instinct to get small, to get gone, to fold into the stone and make no sound. That Cirilo did the opposite — stood up in the road, in the daylight, on the mound, under the lights, where the whole country could see him reach — was the great defiance of his life and the great grief between him and his father; and both of them were right, which is the worst and truest kind of quarrel there is: the kind where love and fear want the same boy to do opposite things, and neither of them is wrong, and only the world that made the quarrel is.

### Chapter — The Tannery Floor

By the time Don Aurelio was an old man, the new Santa Cruz had risen entire on the grave of the old. Lime kilns smoked on the hills where the cattle had grazed; the long wharf walked out into the bay; the Powder Works thumped down the river and the tanneries steamed along the creek, and strangers' money was everywhere, and the family's name was on nothing at all. The language went indoors. The rosary stayed out — that one act of defiance, kept up in the window where the street could see it, the last unsold acre of the rancho.

And on a winter morning in 1882, Don Aurelio's son Esteban — named for the silent Ohlone vaquero who had gentled horses on land the family no longer owned — walked down off the hill and through the gate of Otto Kron's tannery to do, for a Prussian's wages, the one thing the Méndez had always known how to do: turn hide into leather. Only now the hide was another man's, and the leather was another man's, and the lime that had once tanned the family's own kingship into boots and harness only ate, slowly, at a hired man's hands. *They left us our hands,* the old man would say in the years after, to anyone who would sit still for it. *So we gave them to the leather.* It was the closest he ever came to telling the whole story, and most people thought it only an old man's saying, and let it pass.

That same morning, in the back room where the women waited and Dolores held the basin, a child was born — wet and red and roaring, the way a new hide comes off the beam. They wiped him clean, and the old man, ninety years from the kingdom and twelve from his own grave, bent over the basket and took the small hands in his and turned them in the gray light coming through the tannery window. Already big. Already strong. And he did not yet know what they would do, only that whatever the courthouse and the conquest and the long bleeding century had carried off from this family, they had not, somehow, carried off *this* — the hands were unbroken, and they were the family's own, and no lawyer's briefcase and no squatter's fence and no commissioner's four-year patience had ever yet managed to carry a man's hands away from him while he lived.

The family was not finished. Not while there was a Méndez with hands.

And so the whole long story that follows — the boardwalk and the booth and the girl who laughed; the strikeout and the climb and the man in the derby; the cold flat in Brooklyn and the great roaring autumn and the fever that came in on the cheering — all of it begins here, on a tannery floor, in the shadow of a stolen rancho, with a dying old man holding a newborn's hands up to a gray window and refusing, one last time, against ninety years of his own hard evidence, to believe that his family had been born to be second.

His name was Cirilo. Everything that mattered, in the end, was in the hands.

---

## _Siempre Segundo_ — Act I, novelized (full chapters)

### Chapter — Kron

Otto Kron owned the tannery that owned Esteban Méndez, and the truth of Otto Kron — the inconvenient, shades-of-grey truth a simpler story would not trouble with — is that he was not a cruel man, was in some ways a decent one, and that it did not matter nearly as much as decency is supposed to.

He had come from Prussia in '52 with a trade in his hands and nothing else, a tanner's son who had read the word *California* in a Hamburg broadsheet and crossed an ocean and a continent on the strength of it — and arrived to find, as the dispossessed Californios were finding in the same years from the other direction, that the golden country ran on a simpler principle than the broadsheets admitted: that it belonged to whoever got there in time to take it, and that *in time* meant after the Indians and the Mexicans had been cleared off it and before the next wave of hungry men arrived to compete. Kron got there in time. He bought tannery land cheap — land that had been rancho land, Méndez land, a generation before, though he neither knew nor would have cared, having purchased it fair from the American who had purchased it foul — and he built, with genuine skill and genuine sweat, a genuinely good tannery, and worked beside his own men in the early years, and was proud of the work, and the pride was not entirely misplaced. He paid a wage. In the country of that time, paying a wage at all to the men the country had just robbed was, God help us, a kind of progress, and Otto Kron knew it, and took for himself a quiet credit that was both real and obscene — the way so much of that country's decency was both at once.

He was good to Esteban, in the way of the time: which is to say he kept him on for forty years, gave him the better bench, learned his name and used it, and at Christmas pressed a coin into the hands of the Méndez children with a heavy Prussian kindness that was perfectly sincere; and which is also to say that he paid Esteban a fraction of what the work was worth, never once moved him past the floor, and would have been genuinely, woundedly astonished to be told that the warm coin and the long employment and the remembered name were only the velvet on a thing that was, underneath, simply the orderly transfer of a broken man's whole working life into another man's ledger. He did not see it. He was a good man as the word was priced in that country and that trade, and the price was low, and he paid it gladly and thought himself generous — and when Esteban's gifted boy threw a baseball in a way that drew Eastern scouts, Otto Kron was, in his own front parlor, sincerely glad for the family, and said so, and meant it, and never once connected the boy's long impossible climb to anything he himself had done or failed to do; for the tannery and the diamond were, to Otto Kron, simply different countries, and he had no map that showed the road of hides running straight from his own cheap-bought land to the stitched ball in the boy's hand. He died respected, mourned, and solvent, a pillar of the German Lutheran congregation, buried under a good stone in the Protestant ground across town from the small markers Dolores tended — and the leather he had spent his life turning out went on into the world and became, among ten thousand other things, the cover of a baseball and the pocket of a glove, and he never knew that either, and would not have understood why it mattered.

### Chapter — The Boardwalk

The town had a new face that summer, and it wore it down at the water.

Where for fifty years there had been only sand and the long wharf and the fishing smacks, a man named Swanton was building a dream on pilings — a pleasure pier of fresh-cut planks and white paint, a casino strung with a thousand electric bulbs against the blue, a bathhouse, a carousel coming by rail from the East: the whole bright apparatus of a town that had decided to stop making its money from hides and lime and redwood and start making it from joy. They drove the pilings into the exact ground the Méndez cattle had once come down to drink — which is to say they built it, like everything in that town, on land that had been taken — and to build it they hired the sons of the people they had taken it from, and the Italians off the lime kilns, and the Portuguese off the whaleboats, and anyone at all with a strong back and no choice in the matter. Cirilo had driven pilings beside a cheerful, exhausted young Italian named Nunzio Sabatini, who sang while he worked, whose hands were already shiny-burned from a season at Cowell's kilns, and who told Cirilo that in this country a man like them could have anything in the world he wanted, anything, so long as he did not mind being hated for it. Nunzio meant it as a joke. It was the truest thing anyone said to Cirilo that whole year.

By midsummer the pier was open and Cirilo had been promoted, if that was the word, from the pilings up to the planks, and he worked the bright deck of the very thing his own hands had built, among people who would never once wonder whose hands had built it. He was eighteen, and he had long since mastered the one essential skill of his position, which was not to look at the summer people. You learned it the way you learned not to touch the lime vats — once, and never again. The summer people were Anglo and moneyed and down from the City, and they moved through the world Cirilo swept as though it had been arranged for their convenience, which it had; and a brown boy who looked at them too long, or too directly, was asking for a kind of trouble that did not trouble itself to announce its arrival. So he kept his eyes on the work. He was looking, that afternoon, at a pyramid of iron milk-bottles and a worn baseball in his hand, behind a banner that read TEST YOUR ARM — THREE BALLS A NICKEL, when the girl laughed.

It was not the laugh they used on the pier, the bright tin one the young ladies kept in their purses beside their handkerchiefs. It came out of her sideways and surprised, as though he had knocked it loose along with the bottles — which he had, all six of them, blown clean off the shelf by a ball thrown without any windup at all, after the young man beside her in the white flannels had missed three times and then announced, loudly, that the game was rigged and the bottle boy a part of the rig.

He had not meant to throw it. That was the thing he would turn over for years afterward, lying awake: a lifetime of not-looking, of keeping small, of swallowing the Ashbys of the world whole and in silence — and then one young fool in flannels had called him a cheat in front of a girl, and something in Cirilo that he had kept on a short chain his entire life had simply, for the length of one heartbeat, slipped it, and picked up a baseball, and answered. The bottles were still ringing on the planks when he understood what he had done, which was that he had been *seen.*

"Do it again," she said.

"Costs a nickel," said Cirilo, and did not smile, and watched her hold his eyes a beat too long — longer than was safe, longer than was wise — and watched the young man in the flannels watch her do it, and watched the young man begin, in that exact moment, to hate him: with the particular hatred of a man who has just discovered that the thing he believes he owns has a mind of its own, and a preference, and that the preference is not him.

"Then here's a nickel," she said, and held out a coin in a kid-leather glove so soft it might have been sewn by his own father in another life, in another century, when leather still belonged to them. He took it. He gave her a ball. And he understood, with the flat cold clarity of a thing decided somewhere above his head, that the silence he had worn his whole life as the only armor the world could not use against him had just, for the first time, begun to cost him more than it had ever once saved.

### Chapter — The Boardwalk People

The tourists saw the boardwalk. Cirilo, who worked it, saw the people who *were* the boardwalk — the great unseen immigrant crew who built the fun and ran the fun and cleaned up the fun and went home, after the bright crowds left, to rooms as poor as his own; and they were a country unto themselves, the boardwalk people, and they took the bottle boy in as one of their own, because that is what the used do for each other when the using stops at dusk.

There was old Brauer, the German who ran the carousel — the beautiful hand-carved Looff horses, the brass ring, the band organ — and who tended those wooden animals with a watchmaker's tenderness and wept, privately, the day a splintered one was carted off, because he had come from a country that made beautiful things and had landed in one that mostly made money, and the horses were the only beauty his hands got to keep. There was the bathhouse man, and the woman who pulled the saltwater taffy in long shining ropes and had arms like a stevedore from forty years of it; there was the barker at the diving-platform show and the girl who rode the diving horse off the high board into the tank twice a day for a wage that did not match the broken bones, smiling, always smiling, because the smile was the job; there were the freak-show performers, billed as monsters, gentle and bookish and bored between shows, who played cards with the bottle boy and treated him with the easy courtesy of people who knew exactly what it was to be stared at and sorted. And there was Pereira, the Portuguese night watchman, the widower who walked the dark planks till dawn because the rooms he went home to were louder in their silence than the whole Pacific — who never once turned his lamp on the dark wedge under the pier where he knew two young people stood, because he had buried his own and had no wish left in him to ruin anyone.

They were the machinery of other people's golden summers, these people, and they knew it, and they were not bitter about it, mostly, which was the thing that moved Cirilo most and that he carried east: that you could spend your life being the unseen hands behind someone else's joy and still find, among the other unseen hands, a warmth and a courtesy and a card game and a kind of family — that the people the bright crowd looked straight through had quietly built, in the shadow of the very machine that used them, a small decent society of their own, with its own rules, the first of which was that you did not, ever, do to one of your own what the tourists did to all of you. The bottle boy threw a ball through six iron bottles for the rich kids' nickels by day. By night, in the off hours, Brauer let him ride the carousel alone, around and around on a hand-carved horse in the dark with the band organ off and only the sea for sound — a poor brown boy on a rich child's toy in the one hour it was his — and it was, he thought later, one of the kindest things anyone outside his blood ever did for him, and the man never once mentioned it, because among the boardwalk people the kindness that mattered was the kind you did not bill for.

### Chapter — The House in the Tannery's Shadow

Otto Kron's tannery sat where the creek met the flats, and you could find it in the dark with your nose: the green reek of the soaking pits, the lime that ate the hair off a hide and the skin off a careless hand, the oak-bark liquor steeping in its vats like the world's worst tea, and under all of it the sweet, rotten, animal smell of the thing itself — the smell of death being argued, slowly, into something a man could wear. Esteban Méndez had walked through its gate six mornings a week for the better part of thirty years, and he no longer smelled it.

Kron himself was a Prussian, square and gray, who had come to California with nothing but a trade, and who had been called a foreigner in this town for forty years and would be called one in his grave. He was hard with his men and fair in the narrow Prussian way that keeps its word and counts every cent of it, and he and Esteban had between them the particular wordless respect of two men who both knew, without ever once saying so, that the work was honest and the world that bought it was not. Years back, when Dolores was sick and there was no money, Kron had paid a doctor and docked it from no wage and never mentioned it, and had been short and unkind to Esteban for a month afterward, as if the kindness embarrassed him — which it did. He grew rich on the cracked hands of men he would never have had to his table. Both of those things were true at once. Most things, in that town, were two things at once.

Esteban had been fast once. People forgot it, because he had spent thirty years making them forget it, but as a boy he had been the quickest thing in the flats, faster than any Anglo kid in the county — and there had been an afternoon, which he did not speak of, ever, not even to his wife, when his speed had drawn a crowd, and the crowd had drawn the wrong kind of attention, and a friend of his, a Black boy named Toussaint who could hit a ball into the next county, had answered for that attention with his life, off a low oak limb, while Esteban watched from the culvert where bigger hands had pushed him and held him down. He was twelve. He had been fast only in his sleep ever since. He had learned, that year, the great terrible lesson he would spend the rest of his life trying to teach his son: that the nail standing up is the one that gets hammered, that a man survives by being too small to aim at, that love — real love — sometimes looks exactly like making a boy small enough to live. He believed it the way you believe a thing that once saved your life. And the horror of him, the whole grey horror of him, was that he was not entirely wrong.

Dolores ruled the two rooms and the lean-to from the stove, and her faith was not the lace-collar faith of the mission's front pews but the iron thing underneath it, the thing that had carried her people across deserts and through fevers and out the far side of grief still somehow cooking supper. She had borne five children and buried two of them — a girl and a boy, a single winter apart, in the bad year — in the Catholic ground above the town, and she climbed up to them on Sundays and told no one, not even Esteban, what she said there. She kept the language alive in her children's mouths the way she kept the fire alive in the stove: by tending it when no one was watching. And when Esteban, frightened by some fresh sign of the world's notice, wanted the rosary taken down from the window where the street could see it, she left it up, every time, and he never quite found the words to make her take it down — because they both knew that the rosary in the window was the last acre of the rancho the family still owned, and she would not sell it.

Of the three sons who lived, Luis was the middle one and had taken the weight the eldest dropped: he worked the meat market on Pacific Avenue, married young, counted the family's pennies, and carried his resentment of Cirilo the way a man carries a stone in his boot — quietly, and always, and without ever quite deciding to throw it away, for he loved the boy, and also knew that he, Luis, had folded his own small gift into a butcher's apron so that there would be a roof, and no one had ever asked him to, or thanked him for it. And Abel, the eldest, was the cautionary tale told in the next bed: the best natural athlete of all of them, a wrist like a whip and an eye like a hawk, who had discovered early that a brown boy's gift draws the kind of notice that wounds, and had set about, with a methodical fury, drinking and brawling it into the ground before the world could take it from him on the world's own terms. He would be dead of it before he was forty. In the family's private theology he was the proof of the curse — and also, if you looked at him straight on, simply a frightened man getting out of his own way before anyone else could get there first.

So when the family gathered at night around the single lamp, the whole argument of Cirilo's life was already in the room before anyone spoke a word. His mother at the stove with her two graves and her undefeated God. His father gray with another man's hides and the friend he had watched die. Luis with his stone, Abel with his bottle, the old man in the good chair with his ninety years of evidence. And the boy, eighteen, with boardwalk sawdust on his collar and a girl's laugh still going off inside him like a struck bell, and hands — this was the thing, this was always the thing — hands that did not smell of the pit.

"Kron will take you on in the fall," Esteban said one night, not looking up. He said it the way you offer a man a coat in a storm. "Steady work. A man can feed a family on it."

"I have work, Papá. At the beach."

"You have a season at the beach. Until the summer people go home and the planks belong to the gulls again." And then, lower, the old fear rising under the words like water under a floor: "I want you somewhere they can't put you out on a whim, mijo. Somewhere too small to aim at. That's all I want." And he meant Toussaint, though he would never say the name, and at the stove Dolores went still for a moment, because she could hear the dead boy in her husband's voice as plainly as if he had spoken it.

"You want him where you are," said Don Aurelio from his chair — not unkindly, which made it worse. "Safe. Small. Second."

The table went still. Esteban's jaw worked, and the thing he wanted to say — *I kept you all alive, old man, while you sat in that chair and dreamed about a rancho the lawyers ate* — he did not say, because he was a man who had buried his words a long time ago, beside everything else he had buried.

"Second is alive, viejo," was all he said. "I have buried men who reached." And only Dolores knew how literally he meant it.

The old man did not answer his son; he had learned, across a long life, that you do not win an argument with a frightened man by frightening him further. He looked at the boy instead. "Show me the hand," he said; and when Cirilo crossed and held out his right hand, the old man turned it in the lamplight — big, strong, unmarked by the lime, the only unbroken working hand in four generations of the family — and held it the way you hold a thing found again after you had given it up for lost.

"Your great-grandfather tanned hides with a hand like this and called himself a king," he said. "Your father trims them for a Prussian's wage and calls himself safe — and he is not wrong to, God forgive me; I taught him to be safe, and the teaching kept him breathing, and I have hated myself for it every day for forty years." His thumb moved once over the boy's whole knuckles. "But you. You have the first hands in four generations that don't smell of the pit. Whatever else is true — and much of it is terrible, and I have seen most of it — that is also true. Don't you dare give them back."

And Esteban, who loved his son in the only language the world had ever permitted him to learn, looked at the floor and said nothing, because there is nothing to say to an old man who has decided, against ninety years of evidence and the cooling body of a boy named Toussaint, to hope. But under the table, where no one could see it, his cracked and stained and ruined hand had closed, slowly, into a fist — the old shape, the fast boy's shape, the shape he had forbidden himself for a lifetime — and then opened again, and lay flat against his knee, and was still.

### Chapter — Dolores

The men get the headstones and the box scores and the telegrams; the women get the kitchens — and the kitchens are where it is actually decided whether a family lives. Dolores Méndez kept the kitchen, and so Dolores Méndez kept the family, and almost no one ever noticed, which was exactly how she wanted it and exactly the injustice of it, both at once.

She had come from even less than the Méndez had fallen to: a laborer's daughter out of a Watsonville the history books do not trouble with, a girl who could not read and never learned, and who raised four readers anyway by the simple expedient of deciding that they would read and then making the world rearrange itself around the decision. She had married Esteban at sixteen, when he was already gray inside from the thing he had watched at the culvert, and she had spent forty years learning the exact dimensions of her husband's fear and building a life inside them — a life large enough to raise sons in, in the cramped room a frightened good man's caution allowed. She did it the way water does things: patiently, without announcement, finding the level, wearing the stone.

She had buried two children. A girl, Inés, of the diphtheria, at four; and a boy — little Joaquín, named for the old rancho king who had wept over the grant — of nothing anyone could name, at eleven months, in the same hard winter: two graves in the Catholic ground above the town that she climbed to every Sunday of her life, in every weather, and tended, and spoke to, and told no one what she said. The grief did not break her, because she did not permit grief the room to break things; she gave it its Sunday hour and its tended plot and not one inch more, and the other six days she cooked and washed and taught the language and held the line. People mistook this for hardness. It was the opposite of hardness. It was the most ferocious tenderness the whole story contains — a love so large and so disciplined that it had taught itself to keep functioning while carrying two small coffins, because the living children needed a mother whose hands did not shake; and so her hands did not shake, by an act of will repeated every single morning for forty years.

Her faith was the engine of it, and her faith was not the soft thing the word has since become. It was iron, and old, and entirely without illusion. She did not believe God would protect her children; she had two graves that disproved it, and she was no fool. She believed something harder and more useful: that you tend what is given for exactly as long as it is given, with your whole heart and no guarantees; that the tending is itself the prayer and itself the meaning; and that whether anyone is listening is not your business — your business is the pot, the language, the plot on the hill, and the rosary kept in the window where the street can see that this family has not disappeared and will not. When her son the ballplayer died, three thousand miles away, a champion, of the influenza, she was old, and she received the news the way she received all news: standing at the stove. She finished what she was cooking, because there were still people in the house who had to eat. And then she went up the hill, in the rain, to tell Inés and little Joaquín that their brother was coming home to lie down beside them at last, and that he had done a great thing out in the world, and that she had always known he would, and that she would tend the three of them now together, on her Sundays, for as long as God left her the legs to climb — which He did, a long time; because God, who had taken so much from this woman, seemed to understand that the one thing He could not take, and would not try to, was her refusal to stop tending what was hers.

### Chapter — Mijo

There were, at the height of it, four boys who would answer to *mijo* in that house, and the confusion was a daily comedy the family never tired of and never resolved — because resolving it was never the point. The point was the running.

It went like this. Dolores, or the grandmother, or an aunt — it was always a woman; *mijo* was a woman's word, a word with a kitchen in it — would call from the house: *¡Mijo!* Just the one word, doing the whole work of a name. And from wherever they were — the woodpile, the creek, the roof they were forbidden to be on — would come the thunder of boys: Abel first, usually, being oldest and fastest; then Luis; then little Cirilo, the baby, tripping over his own feet to keep up; and as often as not a cousin or two from the Soto house next door, who answered to it on principle, on the sound theory that a call for *mijo* was a call that might, conceivably, end in food.

And then the argument, every time, the same beloved liturgy. *She called me.* — *She did not, she called ME.* — *You're not even her son, you're a Soto, go home.* — *Mijo means the littlest, and I'm the littlest, so mijo means ME* — this from Cirilo, who at five had already built an entire theology around being the baby of the house. And the women would stand in the doorway with their arms folded and let it run; let the boys shove and protest and stake their claims; because a mother of poor children in a hard country learns to bank the warm moments the way she banks anything scarce, against the lean times she knows are coming — and there is nothing warmer, nothing the lean times can never quite take back, than the sound of every boy you love each certain that he is the one you meant.

*Todos,* Dolores would say at last, when the comedy had run its course. *All of you are mijo. That is the trick of the word.* And she would cuff the nearest head with enormous tenderness and put the whole shouting herd of them to the one chore she had actually called one boy for — and they would do it together, grumbling, shoulder to shoulder, four boys and a word that belonged to all of them at once. Cirilo, who carried so few warm things east with him into the cold years that he could number them on his fingers, carried that one: the dirt yard, the thunder of feet, the indignant faces, his mother's folded arms and the laugh she was fighting to keep behind them, and the enormous fact, re-established daily and beyond appeal, that in that house there was always more than enough *mijo* to go around. He was El Halcón to the men — for the hawk, and the gift, and the danger that rode with both. But he was *mijo* to the women, along with his brothers, undifferentiated, interchangeable, beloved in a heap. And on the worst nights of his life, three thousand miles from that doorway, it was never the great name he reached for in the dark. It was the small one. It was the one that meant he had once belonged to a houseful of women who would have fought the whole world bare-handed for any boy who came running when they called.

### Chapter — The Poppies

For a few weeks each spring the dry hills above the town caught fire without burning — the California poppies opening all at once across the slopes, a gold so sudden and so total that the Spanish had called the flower *copa de oro,* the cup of gold, and had named the whole coast, some said, for the way the hills blazed with it in April; and the boy loved the poppies, and the poppies taught him, without his noticing, the shape of his own life.

For the gold did not last, and that was the point of it. The poppy is built for brevity: it throws everything it has into one brief radiant bloom and asks nothing of permanence, and it will not even give you the gold on demand — the flower closes its cup at night and stays shut all through the gray of fog and cloud, opening only to direct sun, so that on the overcast mornings that ruled that coast the hills held their fire furled and secret and gave it up only to the clear bright hours, which were few, and which the poppy did not waste. A few weeks. A few clear hours within those weeks. And then the gold went to seed and the hills browned for the long dry summer and you would not have known, looking at the tawny slopes in August, that they had blazed at all — except that the seed was in the ground, waiting, the way the chaparral's seed waited, for its hour to come round again.

He did not think about it as a boy beyond loving it. But at the end, in the cold East, in the gray that never broke into the kind of clear that opened poppies, he thought about the hills of home going gold for their few clear hours and understood that he had been a poppy all along: a thing built to furl its gold through the long gray obscurity and throw it open, complete and total and brief, only when the clear hour finally came — and that his one clear hour had come at last, the bright autumn of being first, the gold thrown open across the whole slope of his life for everyone to see — and that the poppy does not mourn the brevity, because brevity was never the tragedy; the unopened cup is the tragedy, the gold furled and hoarded and never once thrown to the sun. He had thrown his. Late, and briefly, and at the cost of everything — but he had opened the cup, full gold, in the one clear hour he was given, across the whole hillside of his life, where his people could see it. The fog took it back fast, the way the fog always does. But the hills had blazed. Everyone who looked had seen the gold. And the seed was in the ground.

### Chapter — The Quail

There were California quail all through the brush above the houses, and the boy loved them, and only understood why when he was grown and far away and lonely in a manner the quail could have explained to him in a single morning if he had still been a boy to listen.

They moved as a covey, always, a soft gray crowd of them with the absurd little black topknots nodding as they ran, and they had a system, and the system was the whole of their genius and the boy's whole family in feathered miniature: while the covey fed in the open, scratching and murmuring, one bird — usually a cock — took the high post, a fence rail or a stump or a low bough, and stood sentinel, and watched the sky for exactly the thing the boy would be named for, the shadow of the hawk. And they talked. They talked the entire time, a constant low conversation, *chi-ca-go, chi-ca-go,* a call and an answer passing back and forth across the covey without pause — not because they had anything to say but because the calling *was* the saying; because as long as every bird could hear every other bird answer, no bird was lost, and the moment a voice failed to answer the whole covey knew, and froze, and the sentinel screamed his alarm and they burst up together in a roar of wings. They survived by never once going quiet on each other. They survived by the answering.

That was his family — the constant low call-and-answer of a poor household keeping track of all its own, *¿dónde está? aquí estoy, here I am, I'm here,* the endless small unglamorous verbal touching by which the poor and the hunted keep from losing one another in the dark. He did not know, watching the quail at ten, that he was learning the thing that would nearly kill him at thirty-six: that a bird alone, off on the deep-water road where no covey calls and no one answers and no sentinel watches your particular sky, is a bird in mortal danger, however strong its wings. In Brooklyn, in the cold, in the silence of a flat where no voice called *aquí estoy* back across the dark, he remembered the quail. He had been raised in the answering. The East had no answering in it. And a quail that cannot hear the covey will hold too still, too long, and forget that it was ever part of anything that watched the sky for it — which is one way, the quiet way, that the strong ones go down.

### Chapter — The Hill

The first thing his mother ever taught Cirilo about being a Méndez, she taught him on the hill, among the dead.

He was six, perhaps seven, the first time Dolores took him up — not into the living town but above it, to the Catholic ground where the graves looked down on the bay, where two small markers stood that he had not, until that morning, known were his to mourn. *This is your sister Inés,* his mother said, in the language she would speak only indoors, and here, in the open among the dead, where the street could not hear and it did not matter. *She was four. The diphtheria took her. And this is your brother Joaquín. He was a baby. He did not stay.* She did not weep — he would learn, growing up, that she had a place for the weeping and it was not here; here was for the tending — and she set the boy to pulling the weeds from around the markers with his small hands, and showed him how, and they worked together in the wind off the water; and she told him, in the old language, the thing that was the whole of her religion and the whole of the family's survival folded into one instruction a child could follow:

*We tend them, mijo, because they were ours, and because tending is how you say a thing was real. The world down there* — she meant the living town below, the Anglo town, the courthouse and the boardwalk and the men who had taken the rancho — *that world wants us to disappear. Not to die, exactly. To disappear. To stop having ever been here. And the only answer there is to that is this* — and she laid his small hand on his dead sister's name — *you tend. You keep the name. You say it out loud, and you teach your children to say it, and you do not let the wind or the weeds or the men down the hill take a single one of us off the face of the earth without a fight. That is what it is to be a Méndez. We are the ones who do not disappear.*

He did not fully understand it at six. He understood it at the booth, and on the mound, and in the clubhouse where they handed him a name that was not his; he understood it best of all, perhaps, in a Brooklyn flat at the very end, folding his father's glove into his son's hands — that everything he had ever done, all the reaching and the climbing and the refusing to be made small, had been only the boy on the hill, grown large, pulling the weeds from around the family name so the world could not take it off the earth; that his whole life had been his mother's lesson, played on a bigger hill, before a bigger crowd, in the same wind. And when they brought him home at the end and laid him down, it was on that hill, beside Inés and the first little Joaquín, where his mother could tend all three of her reachers together — for she had decided, standing there, that the ballplayer had been a reacher too, the biggest of them, and that reaching was only another word for refusing to disappear. So she tended him the way she had taught him to tend: pulling the weeds from around his name, in the same wind off the same water, on the same hill where she had once laid his small hand on his sister's stone and told him what it was to be a Méndez. The lesson and the life had become the same thing. They had always been the same thing. She had simply been the first to know it, and he the last, and the hill the place where both of them, in their different decades, finally understood.

### Chapter — The River

In summer the San Lorenzo was the gentle green thing the brothers fished, low and clear over its stones; but the boy learned early not to trust its summer face, because every few winters the same river became a brown destroying god, and took back, in a night, whatever the flats had been foolish or poor enough to build in its way.

The rains would come down off the mountains for days, and the redwoods could only drink so much, and the creeks that fed the river all filled at once, and the San Lorenzo rose out of its bed and went looking for its old wider channels — the ones the town had paved and platted and pretended were streets — and it found them, every time, because a river remembers its floodplain long after the people who build on it have chosen to forget. It came through the flats brown and full of broken things, fence rails and chicken coops and somebody's table, climbing the doorsills in the dark while families carried what they could up to the higher ground and watched the water take the rest; and when it drew back down it left the mud and the stink and the ruin, and the flats people, who had no insurance and no higher ground to move to, shoveled out the mud and rebuilt in the exact same place, because the floodplain was the only ground the poor were allowed to have — the cheap wet dangerous bottomland nobody with a choice would take, which was therefore the land left for the people without a choice.

It taught the same lesson the tide pools taught and the steelhead taught and the whole coast kept teaching: that the forces which periodically wiped the poor were not malice, were not even attention, were simply the river being a river, the indifferent enormous engine going about its business of carrying the mountains to the sea; and that the people who lived in its path did so not from foolishness but from a poverty that had run out of other ground; and that they rebuilt, every time, in the mud, in the same doomed spot, because rebuilding was the only verb left to them — the same stubborn refusal the limpet made against the surf and Dolores made against the graves, the holding-on that is not stupidity but the only dignity available to those the world has assigned to the dangerous ground. The river gave them their water and their fish and their green summer hours, and every few winters it came and took its toll in mud and sometimes in lives, and went back down to the sea, and rose again as fog over the ridge, and fell again as rain, and came down again, world without end; and the flats shoveled out, and rebuilt, and stayed — because staying, on the ground you are given, however dangerous, however often it takes from you, is its own hard form of *we do not disappear.*

### Chapter — The Creek

The best hours of his boyhood, the ones he weighed everything against for the rest of his life and found everything wanting, were the hours the three brothers spent on the San Lorenzo, in the green shade where the creek ran under the alders, doing nothing the world would ever pay for or record.

They went up past the tannery and the smell of it, up where the water ran clean over the stones, and they fished — for the steelhead in the deep pools, and for the trout, with line their mother could ill spare and hooks they guarded like gold — but the fishing was only the excuse, the way the chore is only the excuse for the yard. The real thing was the three of them together in the cool: Abel, the oldest, the gifted one, who moved through the world with a grace that made you ache because you could already tell the world had no place to put it; and Luis, the middle one, who fell in at least once a trip and had to be hauled out spluttering and indignant; and Cirilo, the youngest, watching, always watching, the round gold eye of him already taking everything in. Abel could read the water the way Cirilo would learn to read a hitter — knew which pocket behind which rock a fish would hold in, could lay a line on the current soft as a landing fly — and when he hooked a big sea-run steelhead, fresh up from the salt and still bright with it, the three of them would lose their minds with joy, all the careful quiet gone, three boys hollering in the redwood shade with a silver fish flashing on the line.

And then Abel, who had a tenderness in him the world would later mistake for weakness and break him for, would more often than not work the hook free with his careful hands and hold the great fish a moment in the current, facing it upstream, letting the water run through its gills until the strength came back into it — and let it go. *Let it make the climb, hermanito,* he would say. *It didn't come all this way up from the sea to die in our pan.* And the fish would hang there a heartbeat, gathering itself, and then bolt on up the creek toward the high gravel; and Cirilo, ten years old in the green light, watched his brother give the climbing thing back its climb, and did not yet know that he was watching the truest and most generous act of a life that the country would not let amount to anything — Abel, who let the steelhead go because he understood the drive to get home up the current better than any of them, being himself a steelhead the falls would beat. Those hours cost nothing and earned nothing and were, Cirilo decided at the very end of his life in a cold room three thousand miles east, the richest he was ever given. Just the creek, and the shade, and his brothers, and a bright fish let go to make its climb. There is no box score for it. There never will be. It was the wealth the country could neither give them nor take away, and they had it in abundance, up the San Lorenzo, before the world got hold of any of them.

### Chapter — The First Time He Threw

He was ten the first time anyone understood what was in his arm, and it frightened them, which was the correct response, and which he did not understand until much later.

It was a rock, not a ball — the family could not always spare a ball — a smooth river stone; and there was a tin can on a fence post a long way off, the kind of distance grown men missed, and Cirilo, ten years old and bored, picked up the stone and threw it without thinking and knocked the can spinning off the post on the first try, and then did it again, and again, until his brothers stopped their own game to watch. And Abel — Abel, the gifted one, the one who was *given,* who at twelve could already do things with a ball that drew crowds — Abel watched his little brother throw, and did not say *good arm,* and did not laugh, and did not challenge him to do it again. Abel went quiet: the particular quiet of a boy recognizing, in someone he loves, the thing that is going to hurt them — because Abel already knew, at twelve, having learned it the hard way, what a gift like that drew down on a brown boy in that county.

But it was the old man's face Cirilo remembered. Don Aurelio had been sitting in the shade, ancient even then, and had watched the whole thing — the stone, the can, the impossible accuracy of it, the casual murderous ease — and the boy looked over at his abuelo expecting the thing a boy expects, the pride, the smile, the *that's my grandson;* and instead found the old man regarding him with an expression he had no name for at ten and understood completely at thirty-six on a mound in Boston: fear and love and grief, all at once — the look of a man who has just watched his grandson hand him a thing that is both the family's only hope and its oldest danger, a man doing sums in his head that come out, every way he adds them, to a cost. *Put it down, mijo,* the old man said, very quietly, not unkindly.
