And then, contradicting himself, contradicting ninety years of his own hard-learned caution, in the same breath, because he could not help it, because hope is the thing in us that does not listen to evidence: *No. No — pick it up. Pick it up and throw it again. Let me see it once more, before I tell you to be careful with it the rest of your life.* And the boy threw the stone again and knocked the can spinning, and the old man closed his eyes — the first time, the first of many, the same closed eyes that would decline to watch a championship thirty years on — and Cirilo, ten years old, did not understand why his abuelo, having just seen him do a wonderful thing, looked for all the world like a man at a graveside. He understood it later. He understood it, in the end, completely: the gift and the danger were the same gift; they always had been; the old man had seen it in one throw of a river stone and had been right — and had told him to pick it up again anyway, because that too, the telling-him-to-pick-it-up-anyway knowing the cost, was love. Was, in fact, the whole of what the family had instead of safety. Was the only inheritance they had left to give.

### Chapter — The Fog

The fog was the first thing and the last, and the truth of it — which he understood only at the end — is that Cirilo Méndez was a child of the fog and a man of it, and died in a country that had none, which may be the whole of why he died.

It came in off the water most mornings of his childhood, the way it had come in off that water for ten thousand years before there was anyone on the beach to be hidden by it: the cold Pacific breathing its breath up over the warm land and condensing into a gray country that erased the bright one each dawn and burned off by noon, or did not. The redwoods up the ridge lived on it — could not have lived without it — combing it out of the air with their needles and drinking it down their thousand-year throats through the rainless summer, so that beneath the biggest trees it rained when there was no rain, a private weather the giants made for themselves out of the sea's own breath. Everything in that country was drinking the fog. The boy watched.

He loved it the way you love the thing that hides you. In the fog the boardwalk lights were halos and the barkers' voices came from nowhere and a brown boy and the wrong girl could stand close at the rail and not be seen to stand close; in the fog the lines the daylight drew so sharply — this side, that side, your kind, ours — went soft and gray and negotiable; in the fog, for one hour, before the sun came and sorted everyone again, the world forgot to keep its terrible accounts. He learned the crueler half of it later: that the fog which hides you also erases you; that the same gray mercy that let him stand unseen beside Rebecca was the same gray that hid his whole impossible gift from the men back East who decided who was real. They called the Coast League the fog league, half in mockery, and he pitched the best ball of his life inside that mercy and that erasure both, for years, where the hard sun of the East never reached to see it.

And here is the thing he came to believe at the very end, and said to no one but Rebecca, in the dark: that he had spent his whole life inside one fog or another — the gray off the bay, the *Spanish-not-Mexican* fog the papers wrapped him in, the merciful obscurity of the leagues that would not call themselves major — and that the single autumn the fog finally lifted, the autumn the whole bright country turned and saw him clearly, standing alone and first on the mound in the clear hard Eastern light with no gray left to hide in, was the autumn it killed him. The fog had been keeping him all along. He had hated it for hiding his gift, and he had been wrong; the hiding *was* the keeping. And when at last he stepped out of it into the full sun of being seen, of being first, of being known — the one thing he had wanted his whole life — the clear air, which keeps no one's counsel and grants no mercy, took him inside a month. A child of the fog should never have trusted the clear. But the clear is what they all wanted, him most of all; and wanting it is not a sin; and it killed him anyway — the way the noon sun burns the gray off the bay as though it had never been, while up on the ridge the giants go on, even now, quietly drinking what is left of it, and standing, and standing, and outlasting the light.

### Chapter — Aunt Adeline

Rebecca's mother was dead — the fever, when Rebecca was eight, the careless fever that was not supposed to touch people who could afford doctors and took her anyway — and into the hole that left came Aunt Adeline, Horace's unmarried sister, to raise the girl and run the white house on the bluff; and Aunt Adeline loved Rebecca, in her starched and anxious way, and spent fifteen years doing her quiet best to make Rebecca's life turn out exactly as wrong as her own had.

For Adeline had been a girl once, with her own summer and her own forbidden wanting — there had been a music master, decades gone, a poor and unsuitable man with beautiful hands, and a season when Adeline had felt the whole apparatus of her life crack open toward something real — and her family had managed her out of it, gently, reasonably, with a firmness she had been too well-raised to fight, and married the music master off to distance and married Adeline to nothing at all; and she had spent the long correct remainder of her life as the useful unmarried sister, the aunt, the manager of other people's households, with a small permanent grief where her own life ought to have been. And she had drawn from that grief the conclusion the wounded so often draw: not that the managing had been the crime, but that the *wanting* had been the danger — that safety was the only love that lasted, and that the great kindness you could do a girl was to school the wanting out of her before it could ruin her the way it had so nearly ruined Adeline. She set about saving Rebecca by making her safe, which she had decided meant making her correct.

So they went to war, daily, lovingly, over everything — and most visibly over the clothes. The bathing costume was too daring (an inch of ankle, a bared shoulder, *you are not parading down to that beach got up like a chorus girl, Rebecca, what would your poor mother*); the necklines wanted raising, the hems wanted lowering, the whole young body of the girl wanted covering and managing and keeping, because a body seen was a body wanting was a body in danger, and Adeline knew the road that started at a bared shoulder and where it ended, having walked the first steps of it herself a lifetime ago. And it was not only the clothes. It was the company — which families Rebecca might call on and which she mightn't, which girls were suitable and which were fast, which young men of which fortunes were worth a veranda afternoon (the Ashby boy, above all, the Ashby boy was worth any number of afternoons). It was the hours, the accomplishments, the piano practiced and the calls paid, the whole marriage-market schedule run with the rigor of a railway timetable, all of it pointed at the single station the line had always been pointed at: a correct and comfortable and loveless match, the only kind Adeline had been taught to believe a woman survived.

She was not a villain. That is the thing to hold about her. She was a frightened, wounded, genuinely loving woman trying to spare her niece her own exact fate by the only method she had ever been given, which was the very method that had ruined her — passing the wound forward, dressed as protection, the way the wounded so often do; and once, only once, brushing out Rebecca's hair on a summer night with the window open and the boardwalk music coming faint up the bluff, she came within a breath of telling the girl the truth, of saying *there was a man, once, with beautiful hands, and I let them talk me out of him, and I have been cold ever since, and I am so afraid you will be warm and I am so much more afraid you will be cold like me* — and did not; and the not-saying-it was its own small tragedy, one of the thousand uncarved tragedies stacked in that white house on the bluff, where everyone loved Rebecca and no one, not one of them, had the first idea how to let her be free.

### Chapter — The Girls

The boardwalk was where the girls got loose, for a few stolen golden hours, from the whole heavy apparatus of being turned into wives; and Rebecca's set went down to it every summer afternoon they could engineer, and the social warfare they waged on those sunny planks was every bit as intricate and as merciless as anything their fathers conducted in the banks.

It had a queen, the way these things always have a queen, and the queen was Florence Pryor — beautiful, quick, and armored in a confidence Rebecca half-envied and half-saw straight through — who kept the hierarchy and decided, by a tilt of her parasol, who was in the set that season and who had become, mysteriously, impossible. There was Cora Beck, soft and loyal and a little plain, who was Rebecca's actual friend, the one she told the true things to; and there were the others, the satellites, arranging and rearranging themselves around Florence in alliances and freeze-outs and coded little cruelties that were, when you stood back from them, the identical caste system the grown people ran, rehearsed in miniature by sixteen-year-olds in summer muslin — who walked with whom along the rail, who was asked to whose veranda, whose bathing costume was admired and whose was *talked about,* the whole pitiless sweet machinery of belonging. Florence had set her cap at the Ashby boy and his moneyed set, and measured every other girl against herself in beaux and frocks and accomplishments, and was, under the dazzle, as frightened as any of them of ending up on the wrong veranda; they all were; the fear was the engine the whole thing ran on, even as they shrieked down to the water with their arms linked, luminous and laughing, sixteen and free for one warm hour.

And the freedom was real, for that hour. Down past the bathhouses they went, into the daring costumes Aunt Adeline's horror had been left behind up the bluff to fret over, into the cold bright surprising sea — and there was the warmth after, the towels, the salt drying on the skin, the inside jokes, the sidelong watching of the boys who watched them back, the first electric ungovernable stirrings of being young and lovely on a summer coast with the whole apparatus momentarily out of sight. It was the best of their lives and some of them sensed it and most did not. Rebecca sensed it, and could not enjoy it, and that was her particular trouble: she was the most looked-at girl in the set and the least content, played the whole game beautifully and felt its hollowness in her teeth, went through the bright motions with a private starvation she had no name for — the motherless girl, looked at by everyone and into by no one, performing belonging on the warm planks while something in her quietly went hungry.

Which is why it landed the way it did, the small thing, the thing none of the other girls even registered. They had stopped at the baseball booth — Florence's idea, to watch the Ashby boy throw and admire him for it — and the bottle boy behind the counter was brown and poor and beneath their notice, and one of the boys flicked a remark at him about his accent and Florence laughed her bright cutting laugh, and the moment was nothing, the moment was the most ordinary moment on the whole boardwalk. But Rebecca, watching, saw the bottle boy take it — saw him keep his face still and his eyes level and absorb the little cruelty without flinching, with a dignity that had clearly been practiced because it had clearly been needed — and then saw him glance, just once, at her; and it was not the way the Ashby boy looked at her, which was the way you look at a prize you intend to win, and it was not the way the men on the verandas looked at her, which was the way you appraise a property; it was the way you look at a *person,* level and curious and entirely unimpressed by her dress or her name, as though he had seen the hungry thing inside the lovely thing and was simply, quietly, interested in it. It lasted a second. Florence was already pulling them away. And Rebecca walked on up the planks with her friends, laughing at the right places, and could not, for the rest of that strange unsettled afternoon, get the one look out from under her ribs — the first crack, though she did not know it yet, in an apparatus that had been built, with such love and such care and by so many frightened hands, to make absolutely certain she never felt exactly this.

### Chapter — The Dark Under the Pier

They could only have each other in the dark, and so the dark became theirs.

When the boardwalk lights went out and the surf came up to fill the silence, they met under the pilings, in the black wedge of shadow beneath the pier — the one place in that whole bright town where a brown boy and a white girl could stand together without it costing blood. Her father believed she was asleep in the rented cottage up on the bluff. His father believed he was a fool, and was right, and was also afraid, which Cirilo did not yet understand were the same thing in his father. They were deceiving everyone who loved them, those nights, and neither of them could make themselves care — and that frightened Rebecca more than the dark did, because she had been raised to believe she was a careful person, and here was proof, nightly, that she was not.

She had come to him, in the beginning, out of something she was honest enough to mistrust in herself. She was motherless — her mother had died when Rebecca was eight, of the same careless fever that took the poor, except that it was not supposed to take people who could afford doctors, and had taken her anyway — and her father had answered the loss by converting his daughter, across a decade, into an ornament: a beautiful, accomplished, marriageable thing to be set out at the right tables and married, in the fullness of time, to the right name. She had spent eleven years being looked *at* — at her dress, her posture, her prospects — and never once, not by her father nor by Chuck Ashby nor by any of the right names at any of the right tables, looked *into.* And then a bottle boy on a pier had knocked six iron bottles off a shelf and turned and *seen* her — not the dress, not the prospects, but the person rattling around loose inside all of it — and the thing it broke open in her was so large and so sudden that she mistook it, at first, for simple rebellion, for the satisfying scandal of wanting the one boy on the beach she had been forbidden. It was only later, much later, in cold rooms three thousand miles to the east, that she would be certain it had been love. On the nights under the pier she was not yet certain, and she knew she was not certain, and she came anyway — which is either the bravest thing a careful person can do or the most reckless, and is generally both at once.

"Tell me something true," she said once. "You never do."

He let the sand run through his fingers a while before he answered. "My family owned this," he said at last. "Not the pier — the land. All of it, the river to the redwoods, before there was a Santa Cruz to own it. Now I sell nickels on a plank built on top of it." He looked at her in the dark. "And I would rather be here, like this, with you, than have had a single acre of it back."

She took his hand — the pitching hand, the unmarked one — and held it like a thing she had decided to keep, and told him they would simply have to take the rest of it back themselves; and just then a whistle came down off the boardwalk and a watchman's lamp swung out over the rail. They froze together in the black while the beam swept the sand above their heads. It was only old Pereira, the night man — a Portuguese widower who had buried a wife and three of his four children, and who walked the empty pier until dawn because the rooms he went home to were louder, in their silence, than the whole Pacific. He did not want to find anyone. He swept the lamp along out of habit and not out of zeal, and the beam passed over the dark wedge where they stood pressed together and breathing, and moved on, and Pereira shuffled away up the planks humming something low in a language neither of them knew — a man wholly uninterested in ruining two strangers, because the world had done enough ruining of its own to last him the rest of his life.

They breathed again. But the fear was its own answer, the thing neither of them ever said aloud: that all of it — the dark, the nights, the held hand — was borrowed, and the lenders were cruel and patient and kept good books, and that somewhere a reckoning was being entered, in a ledger they would not be allowed to read until the day it was presented, in full, for payment.

### Chapter — The Carousel

There was one night that summer that Cirilo set against the whole rest of his life and found the rest of his life, even the championship, even the boy, came up a little short of — the night Rebecca rode the carousel with him in the dark.

Brauer knew. That was the thing about the boardwalk people: they saw everything and billed for none of it. The old German had caught the bottle boy and the bluff girl in the shadow under the pier more than once on his rounds and had said nothing, the way Pereira said nothing, because the boardwalk people had a country of their own and the first law of it was that you did not hand your own kind to the daylight. And on a night near the end of that first summer, with the crowds gone and the lights mostly down and the planks belonging to the gulls, Brauer threw the lever that woke the carousel — the hand-carved Looff horses, the painted manes, the brass ring on its arm — and started the band organ low, and then walked away to the far rail to watch the black water and grant them the one impossible thing the daylight would never give: an hour to be young together, in the open, like anyone.

They rode it alone, the two of them, in the dark, the great wheel of horses turning slow and the organ playing some sweet tinny waltz under the stars and the surf, Rebecca on a white horse with her hand out to the brass ring she kept just missing, laughing the real laugh, the sideways surprised one, not the bright tin purse-laugh of the bluff; and Cirilo on the black horse beside her, reaching out across the gap between the rising-falling horses to hold her hand whenever the painted animals brought them level, losing it, finding it again on the next turn — the whole courtship of them in one image, the reaching across a gap, the brief level holding, the loss, the patient finding-again on the next go-round. For one hour the line did not exist. For one hour there was no bluff and no flats, no Anglo and no Mexican, no Aunt Adeline and no Chuck Ashby and no county with a rope in it — only a boy and a girl and a turning wheel of wooden horses and a German at the far rail keeping their secret and the indifferent merciful sea. *This is what it would be,* Rebecca said once, quietly, on a turn, not looking at him. *If the world were different. This. Just this, going around.* And Cirilo, holding her hand for the length of one level pass before the horses rose and parted them again, said the truest and most dangerous thing he had ever said to anyone: *Then we'll have the hour. They can have the world. We'll take the hour.* And they rode the wheel until Brauer, gently, without a word, threw the lever and let it coast to still — and the hour ended, the way the hour always ends, and the world came back; but he had it, ever after, the black water and the band organ and her hand found and lost and found on the turning, the one hour the line let them steal, the hour he was reaching for, in his heart, on the mound in Boston and in the cold flat at the end: *we'll take the hour.*

### Chapter — The Oldest Brother

The drinking did not come from nowhere, and the family knew exactly where it came from, because they had watched it before, in the old man, a generation up.

Don Aurelio had drunk, in the bad years — the years after the courthouse, after the rancho went, when there was nothing for a deposed Californio with ruined hands and useless dignity to do with the grief but pour it down a hole every night and hope to wake somewhere else. He had come out the far side of it eventually, dried himself around the time the grandchildren came and turned the wreck of himself into the stern watching man on the bucket; but the family remembered, the way families remember, in a lowered voice. And when the same shadow came down over Abel at seventeen, they knew it on sight — the inherited shape of a Méndez man drinking what the country had done to him.

For Abel had the gift, and the gift drew the eyes, and the eyes, in that county, drew the trouble. There was a Sunday he had hit three balls so far and so contemptuously over a club of Soquel and bluff boys that the thing curdled, the way it curdled, from a game into an affront; and after, in the dust by the wagons, a bluff boy named Roy Calder and three others backed him to a fence and explained to him in the reasonable voice, with the casual hands, what happened to a Mexican who made white men look like fools in front of their girls — and Abel, who had his grandfather's pride and not one ounce of his patience, broke Roy Calder's nose with one beautiful instinctive right hand before he could think better of it. It took Luis and two flats men to drag him off. It cost him three weeks in the county jail and a beating from the deputies on the way in, and it taught him at seventeen the lesson the culvert had taught his father: that the arm which made him magnificent was the exact thing that would get him killed, and that there was no safe way in that place to be as good as he was.

So he chose the unsafe way, and then he chose the bottle, and the two became a single habit. He could not stop being the best — it came out of the body whether he willed it or not — and he could not be the best without drawing the trouble, and he could not meet the trouble with his fists without the jail, and he could not bear any of it sober. But there was one use for the fighting the family could never quite condemn, because it was the last noble thing in the wreck of him: he spent it on his brothers. When the bluff boys came for Luis over some banker's daughter, it was Abel who came out of the dark and took the beating onto himself; when a boardwalk clerk shoved little Cirilo off the planks for blocking a doorway, it was Abel who put the clerk through a fruit stand and did another two weeks for it. He could not defend himself in any way that did not ruin him — but he could stand in front of his brothers, and he did, every time, drunk or sober, fists first: the gift turned from a glory into a weapon and from a weapon into a shield. And the jail record grew, and the drinking deepened, and Dolores lay awake, and the family said in the lowered voice, *like the old man, God help him — just like the old man,* and feared, correctly, for where it would end.

### Chapter — The Set

The rich boys had a cage too. It was a better-appointed cage than the one down in the flats — it had lawn tennis in it, and a yacht at the harbor, and a clear paved road to Stanford and the bank — but it was a cage, and the boys in it were as frightened as anyone down in the flats; and the cruelty on the boardwalk only makes its full sense from up on the bluff, seen from inside.

They were the sons of the men who had won: the bankers, the lumber barons, the land men whose fathers or grandfathers had been on the right side of the courthouse when the Méndez and the others were on the wrong. Chuck Ashby was their center of gravity — not the richest, but the surest, the one who never seemed to doubt, which in a boy that age is the rarest currency there is — and around him orbited the others: Roy Calder, who carried his broken nose like a grudge and a banner; Theo Pope, the lumber heir, soft and mean in the way of boys who suspect they are soft; and the rest of them, a loose bright cruel confederacy of seventeen-year-olds who owned the summer the way their fathers owned the town. And here is the thing the flats boys could not see from below: those boys were not free either. They were performing, every waking minute, an enormous and exhausting role — the role of effortless superiority — for an audience of their fathers, and their fathers' approval was a coin doled out only for winning, only for dominance, only for never once showing the doubt or the softness or the fear that all of them carried and none of them could admit; and the performance had a price, and the price was that they could not afford to be kind, because kindness, in their cage, read as weakness, and weakness was the one unforgivable thing.

So when they worked over the bottle boy, the easy mark, it was not, mostly, about him at all. It was about each other. It was a display, a rehearsal of dominance staged for the watching girls and for the watching set and above all for the watching internalized fathers, each boy proving to the others and to himself that he belonged on the top of the heap by demonstrating, on a body it was safe to demonstrate on, that he could put another human being beneath him without a flicker of conscience. The bottle boy was not their enemy. The bottle boy was their *equipment* — the apparatus on which a rich boy proved his rank — which is, if you think about it, a worse thing to be than an enemy, and Cirilo felt the difference and never forgot it: that to be hated would at least have been to be *seen,* and what they did instead was use him, casually, as a thing, a rung, a way of talking to each other over his head about which of them was hardest. Chuck Ashby was the best at it, the most fluent, the one who could land the cut that made the others laugh and the girls go quiet — and Chuck Ashby, alone in his grand cold father's house at night, was also the most frightened of all of them, the one who had learned earliest and most thoroughly that in his family love was another word for winning, and that a boy who stopped winning, even for a moment, even out of mercy, would find the warmth switched off in the great house like a tap. He was not a monster. He was a boy being made into one, with great care, by people who called it raising him. That does not excuse the slap he would shortly lay on the bottle boy's cheek. It only means that when it lands, you should understand that it was a blow struck by a prisoner, for his jailers, against a man he had been carefully taught not to see.

### Chapter — What It Costs

The reckoning came, the first installment of it, on the bright planks in the full light of a Tuesday afternoon.

Cirilo was hauling crates when Chuck Ashby and two of his college friends stepped into his path — easy, grinning, hands in their pockets, with the loose insolence of men who have never once in their lives been outnumbered and so do not recognize that this, three on one, is precisely what outnumbering looks like from the other side. They had been talking about him, Chuck said. About how he'd been *seen.* At night. With a young lady of their mutual acquaintance.

"I don't know what you mean," Cirilo said, and set the crate down, slow, and kept his eyes at the level of the man's collar, which was the level the world permitted.

"You know exactly what I mean." And then Chuck Ashby, who was not, whatever else he was, a stupid man, told him how it went — pleasantly, conversationally, the way one man tells another the weather. Out here, for a fellow like Cirilo, with a girl like that, there was no court and no judge and no arm fast enough in the world. There was a rope, and a tree, and a crowd that went home afterward and slept the sleep of the just. He said it without heat, and that was the worst of it, because heat would have meant it was personal, and the absence of heat meant it was simply *true,* a fact of the country like the tide. He stepped in close. "Throw all the strikes you want, Mendez. You can't strike out a county." And he reached up and patted Cirilo's cheek — once, lightly, an insult measured with a jeweler's precision to be too small to answer and too large, ever, to forget — and turned and strolled off up the planks, laughing at something one of his friends had said, already, in his own mind, finished with the whole matter.

But he was not finished with it, and that was the thing Cirilo could not have known, standing there with his hands shaking. For Chuck had felt, in the half-second his palm rested on the other man's face, the muscle jump in Cirilo's jaw — the held thing, the leashed thing, the enormous and disciplined refusal — and somewhere under the easy cruelty a small cold voice had told Chuck the truth he would spend twenty years declining to hear: that the bottle boy had not answered the insult not because he *could* not but because he had *decided* not to; that the power in the transaction was not, in fact, entirely Chuck's; and that a man who can absorb a slap like that and merely stand there, deciding, is a far more frightening thing than a man who swings. Chuck walked away laughing to cover the fact that, for one instant, on a sunny pier, with two friends at his back and a whole county at his command, he had been afraid.

Cirilo stood very still. Then he picked the crate back up. His hands — the good hands, the unmarked ones, the first in four generations to smell of something other than the lime — were shaking, and not from the weight. He carried the crate into the cool dark of the storeroom and set it down and stood there a while; and he thought of his father, who came home gray every night and wanted only that his son be too small to aim at; and he understood at last, all the way down, the thing the old men had been trying to tell him in two different voices his whole life. The family was not merely cursed to second. It was that *reaching for first* — out loud, in the daylight, where they could see you do it — was a thing this country had ways of charging a man for that had nothing whatever to do with baseball. His father had paid that bill once, in a culvert, at twelve years old. Cirilo stood in the dark and felt the old fear settle over him like his father's own coat, two sizes too big and somehow still warm — and felt, underneath the fear and refusing it, the thing he had thrown that day without meaning to: the arm, the answer, the chain slipping its post. Both things lived in him at once. They would go on living in him at once for the rest of his life.

### Chapter — Don Aurelio's Game

The old man had loved baseball since before most of the country knew it had a name, and his loving it was its own long story, and a sad one under the joy, and it was the reason there was an overturned bucket behind every backstop the boy ever pitched in front of.

Don Aurelio had come up into his manhood in the Gold Rush years, when California filled overnight with men from everywhere, and the men brought their games, and the newest and brashest of them — the New York game, baseball — took to that raw place like fire to dry grass. He had watched it played on bare dirt by miners and sailors and clerks with their sleeves rolled, bare-handed in those first years, no gloves, the catcher standing well back and catching the thing on the bounce; he had seen the first San Francisco clubs form, the Eagles and the rest, grown men in earnest playing a children's pastime with a seriousness that puzzled their elders; and in 1869, a grown man with sons of his own by then, he had stood in a vast California crowd and watched the Cincinnati Red Stockings, the first openly all-salaried nine, the first true professionals, come west on the brand-new railroad and beat the local clubs senseless — and he had understood, watching those red-stockinged men move, that something was being born that would matter, that this game was going to be the country's own, the thing it would tell itself about itself. He fell in love. He memorized it. He gave it the part of his heart a man that age does not usually have left to give.

And it did not love him back — not the way he loved it — because by the time the game finished becoming the great American mirror, it had drawn, like everything American, its line; and a brown man, a Californio, a son of the conquered, could play it on the dirt for nothing and could watch it from the cheap seats and could love it with his whole soul, but the high clean professional version of it, the version with the salary and the train and the crowd, was being built with a door he was on the wrong side of. Don Aurelio learned that the way he learned the loss of the land — slowly, and completely, and without anyone in the room ever doing a thing he could name. So when the river-stone arm appeared in his own grandson two generations later, the old man's joy and his terror were the same thing, because he already knew the whole shape of it: that the boy had been given precisely enough gift to be broken against precisely the door that had broken the old man's own heart for forty years. He sat on the bucket and taught the boy everything anyway — every grip, every read, every trick the bare-handed years had taught him — pouring his entire ruined hopeless lifelong love of the game into the one Méndez who might, might, carry it through the door he himself had only ever pressed his face against. *Learn it all, mijo,* he said, and meant: *let one of us, finally, be too good for them to keep out.* It very nearly worked. That is the joy in it. It very nearly, and then exactly too late and at exactly the wrong cost, worked.

### Chapter — The Charmer

Luis was the beautiful one — not the most gifted, that was Abel; not the one with the arm, that was Cirilo; but the beautiful one, the charmer, the brother who walked into a room and quietly rearranged it — and what he did with that, in the boardwalk summers of his teens, was the single most dangerous thing a Méndez boy in that county could do: he made the rich men's daughters fall in love with him, and he fell in love with them back, every time, with his whole reckless heart.

They found him thrilling precisely because he was forbidden — the bluff girls, the bankers' and the lumber men's daughters, bored and chaperoned and curious, for whom a handsome boy from the flats with a grin like a struck match was the most exciting danger their safe summers had on offer. And Luis, who should have known better, who had his father's culvert and his brother's broken-knuckle lesson right there in front of him, could not help himself, because he was seventeen and on fire and the wanting was simply bigger than the fear. He went over the fences. He learned which bluff gardens had a gap in the hedge and which bathhouse hours the chaperones dozed; he crept onto the great dark lawns to stand under a lit window; he talked his way into dances he had no business at, in a borrowed collar he'd half-stolen the dime for, and got one waltz with a banker's daughter before someone placed his face and the music stopped. There was a girl named Maud Carrow — a whole summer of her — who loved the thrill of him the way you love a thing you fully intend to give back, and met him in the cypress shadows below her father's house, and wept real tears the night it ended, and married a Stanford man in the fall, and did not, so far as anyone ever learned, think of Luis Méndez again; and Luis, who had fallen for her completely, because he fell completely every time — that was his gift and his ruin both — carried that one a long while. He was not a cad. That was the heartbreak of him; a cad would have been safer. Luis actually loved them, the unreachable girls, really loved them, and got his heart broken on the bluff fences over and over for the crime of believing, each time, that this one would be different, that love might somehow be stronger than the line. It never was. The line always won.

And it cost him in blood, because the fathers and brothers of those girls did not take it lightly — a Mexican sniffing at their daughters — and Luis took his beatings for it, in alleys and behind bathhouses, the price of the line collected on his body; and it was Abel, more often than not, who came out of the dark to even the count and went to jail for it; and it was Dolores who lay awake over the pair of them, the drinker and the lover, fearing the rope for the one and the bottle for the other, having already buried two children and being unwilling, with the whole ferocity of her, to bury any more. *That boy is going to get himself killed over a girl who will not even remember his name,* she said of Luis — and she was very nearly right, and the nearness of it is the only reason there was a Luis left at all to become the family's spine.

For the boardwalk summers were only a prologue to the day Luis put it down. When Abel's drinking made it plain the oldest would not carry them, Luis — the charmer, the scandal, the fence-climber — folded the whole beautiful reckless wanting self of him into a butcher's apron, married a flats girl named Soledad who was kind and steady and his own, and never went over another fence as long as he lived. The wildness did not die; he buried it; there is a difference, and he felt the difference every day of the long useful unglorious life that followed. And his little brother Cirilo watched all of it — watched Luis love across the line and bleed for it and break his heart on it and finally bury it alive — and learned, in his own body, years before he ever laid eyes on Rebecca, both halves of the one lesson that would matter most: exactly how much the wanting cost, and that it was going to be, even knowing the cost to the decimal, completely impossible not to want.

### Chapter — The Exhibition

He had only one answer to all of it, and one bright Sunday in the new century he gave it in front of the whole town.

It was a challenge game, got up the way such games were — the local nine against a touring club of college boys down from the City for the weekend, Stanford men in crisp flannels with Chuck Ashby among them, loose and certain and already, in his own mind, the hero of the afternoon. A holiday crowd came out to the ground behind the Leibbrandt baths: parasols and straw boaters, the smell of frying smelt and spilled beer, the boardwalk organ carrying its waltzes over the outfield fence. Down the third-base line the people from the flats had gathered in their own loose knot — the Italians off the kilns, the Portuguese off the boats, the Mexican families who spoke the language only at home — and among them Nunzio Sabatini, who had taken an afternoon off he could not afford; and when Cirilo walked out to take the ball for the locals and the whole college bench began to laugh at the bottle boy from the pier come out in spikes, Nunzio rose and bellowed something joyful and obscene in Italian that made the women swat at him and the men grin.

Then Cirilo threw, and the laughing stopped.

The first college man swung at a fastball that was already in the catcher's mitt. The second got the wet one — the spitball, the dying ball, the pitch his abuelo had pressed on him with a warning folded inside it — and it dropped off the table a foot in front of the plate and left him lunging at air, foolish, in front of his girl and his friends and the whole bright grandstand. And the third was Chuck Ashby, who was not laughing now, who dug in with his jaw set and fouled one back and flailed at two more and finally stalked off to the bench telling anyone who would listen that the ball was doctored — which it was, and which the rules of that year entirely permitted, and which gave him back not one shred of the thing he had just lost in front of the only people he had ever been taught to count. Down the third-base line the flats came apart with joy. Nunzio was weeping with laughter. It was, Cirilo would think much later, the first wholly happy hour of his life, and it was happy precisely because it was *witnessed* — because for one afternoon the thing he could do was larger than the thing he was, and the whole town had to sit in the sun and watch it be larger.

In the little grandstand, a spare man in a dark Jesuit coat had stopped writing in his book.

His name was Aloysius Fagan, and he had come to Santa Clara by way of a hard Limerick boyhood and a harder novitiate, and he carried inside his cassock two appetites he had never managed to reconcile and had stopped, by middle age, expecting to: a genuine and burning love of God and of the boys in his charge, and a frank, competitive, nearly worldly hunger to beat Stanford at baseball every single spring — which he confessed as a sin twice a year and committed the rest of the time with his whole heart. He had seen a thousand arms. He had not seen this one. He watched Cirilo work the way another man might watch a sunrise, and he was honest enough even in the watching to admit he did not entirely know which of his two appetites had stood him out of his seat — and Jesuit enough to suspect it did not finally matter, that God was perfectly able to work His purposes through a baseball coach's vanity, having so little else, in this fallen country, to work with.

After the last out he came down to the fence.

"Son," he said. "Have you given any thought to college?"

"We can't pay for college, Father," Cirilo said.

"No," said Fagan, with a small dry smile that had Limerick still in it. "But Santa Clara can pay for an arm. We play Stanford every spring." He let his eyes drift, just once, to Chuck, sulking white-flanneled beside the charabanc that had carried the college boys down. "Wouldn't you like to do that again — where it counts?"

And the door of Cirilo Méndez's whole life swung open on a single sentence; and on the far side of it, only an hour up the line, was every last thing he had been told, all his life, in two languages and by people who loved him, that he was not permitted to want.

### Chapter — The Condor

There was a greater bird than the hawk over that coast once, the greatest in all of North America, and the coast people had held it sacred; and it was the hawk's dark twin, and it carried the one thing about death that everything else had only circled.

The California condor — ten feet of wing tip to tip, a soaring black cross against the sky, riding the thermals so high and so still it seemed less a bird than a piece of the heavens deciding whether to descend — was a vulture, an eater of the dead, and the coast people did not despise it for that the way the newcomers would; they revered it, gave it a place in the oldest stories, because they understood the thing the condor does, which is the holiest and most necessary thing there is: it takes what has died and lifts it back into the sky. The deer that falls, the seal that washes up, the whale on the beach, the spent steelhead in the eddy — the condor finds them, and eats them, and turns the dead flesh into the highest flight in the hemisphere, so that the fallen thing does not merely rot but *rises,* is carried up on a ten-foot wing into the blue, recycled from death into the most magnificent soaring the continent could show. The hawk is the hunter, the spirit of the reaching life; the condor is its twin, the spirit of what the reaching life becomes — not an end but a lifting, the dead made wing.

The newcomers, of course, nearly killed it — shot it, poisoned it, let the lead of their bullets into the carcasses it fed on, brought the great sacred recycler of the coast down toward the same nothing they brought the otter and the seal and the redwood and the men, until only a remnant rode the far back-country thermals. But the boy, who never saw one — they were nearly gone from his stretch of coast by his boyhood — would have understood the condor at the end if anyone had told him the old stories, because the condor is the answer to the question his own death would pose, about what such a death is for. He died at the summit, spent, like the steelhead on the gravel; and the question is whether that is only an ending — only rot, the gift wasted and the man used up and dropped. And the condor says no. The condor says that what dies at the height does not merely fall; it is taken up, and carried, and turned into flight — that the spent father becomes the glove in the son's hand and the name in the flats' mouth and the gold in the seed in the ground, lifted out of death on a great dark wing into something higher than the living man could reach alone. The hawk had come for him at the start, to mark the reaching life. And the condor, the hawk's sacred twin, was the promise underneath the grief: that the dead, on this coast, in this family, are not dropped. They are lifted. They rise.

### Chapter — Año Nuevo

Up the wild coast north of town, at the point the Spanish had named for the new year, there was a thing the boy saw once and never forgot: the elephant seals, the great absurd magnificent monsters of that shore, hauled out in their hundreds on the sand — and they were, though he could not have known the whole of it then, another verse of the same song the otters sang, the song about being taken nearly all the way down to nothing and refusing, even so, to be quite finished off.

They are enormous, the bulls three tons and more, with the ridiculous pendulous noses that give them the name, roaring their challenges and rearing up to slam their great scarred chests together over the harems in a violence that shakes the beach — and they had been, within living memory, all but gone. The whalers and the sealers had hunted them for their oil the way the fur men had hunted the otters for their pelts, rendered the giants down into barrels of lamp-oil and lubricant, killed them on their own breeding beaches where they could not flee, and killed them and killed them until the species was believed extinct, gone, erased from the coast entirely — and then it turned out that a remnant had survived, a single tiny herd, a few dozen animals on one remote Mexican island that the killers had somehow overlooked, holding on, breeding, waiting; and from that handful, that overlooked unkilled remnant, the whole vast roaring recovery had slowly begun, until the beaches filled again with the impossible three-ton bulls and the harems and the weaned pups, a population brought back from the literal edge of nothing by a few survivors the slaughter had missed.

It was the otters again, and the basket-weaver again, and the chaparral again, and his own people again — the same coast teaching the same lesson in a different enormous shape: that the powerful will take a thing all the way down to what they believe is the end, render it into barrels for a distant market, and write it off as gone; and that *gone* is a verdict the powerful are wrong about more often than they will ever admit, because somewhere there is nearly always a remnant they overlooked, a handful holding on on the ground no one wanted, breeding in the dark, waiting — and that from that overlooked unkilled handful the whole roaring improbable return can come, given only time and the blind enormous engine that does not know how to quit. They had thought the elephant seals gone. They had thought the Californios broken. They were wrong about the seals. The boy looked at the impossible recovered monsters roaring on the beach and felt, without the words for it, that they might yet be wrong about his people too — that *erased* and *overlooked* are not the same word, and that the difference between them is the whole of hope.

### Chapter — The Whales

Twice a year the gray whales went by, close in along that coast, and the whole town knew it the way it knew the seasons of the fish and the fog: the spouts standing up off the water like little gray trees that grew and vanished, the long dark backs rolling, the oldest road on the planet running invisible just offshore.

They were going where they had always gone — down in the winter to the warm lagoons of Baja to bear their young, up in the spring to the cold rich Arctic to feed — the longest migration of any creature that draws breath, ten thousand miles a year, swum by an animal navigating a featureless gray country by means no one could name. And in the spring they came back north past Santa Cruz mothers-and-calves, the great cows keeping their young tucked close to shore, away from the deep water where the killers waited, teaching them the road. That was the part that held the boy at the rail: the calf did not know the way. It had never made the journey. The route lived in the mother, and she swam it with her child pressed to her flank for ten thousand miles, holding it to the safe inshore line, breaching once in a while as if to say *watch, this is how, remember this point, remember the taste of this water* — handing the whole long road forward to a creature that would one day swim it alone, and teach it in turn to a calf of its own, the map living nowhere but in the passing-down, the same as the monarchs, the same as the family, the same as everything that survives at all.

Cirilo watched the spouts go north the spring before he left for good — the season the cows and calves came through — and felt the thing he had no words for at the rail and only understood later: that he was about to swim out into the deep water his own mother could not follow him into, off the safe inshore line, out where the killers were; that no one could press him to their flank for the part of the road he was about to take; that this was simply what the calf must finally do, leave the teaching mother and swim the dangerous open by the map she had put in him, and either make it or not. *Go, mijo,* his mother would say, when the day came, dry-eyed until he had turned away. She had swum him as far up the safe line as she could. The deep water was his. The whales went by, north, mother and child, the oldest road on earth, and then they were past, and the sea closed gray and enormous over where they had been, keeping its counsel, as it had over the otter hunters and the hide ships and would over him.

### Chapter — The Leaving

He left from the little depot in his one good suit — Dolores had turned the collar and pressed it twice the night before, saying nothing, which was how she said the most — with a college letter buttoned into his breast pocket and a valise that did not need to be large.

The family came to the end of the lane but not onto the platform, because the platform belonged to the other Santa Cruz, the one with the money and the timetables, and they had learned where their welcome ended as surely as they had learned where every other line was drawn. Luis shook his hand hard, said something about writing, and looked away — his resentment and his love so thoroughly braided by now that even he could no longer have told you which was which. Abel, already a little drunk at nine in the morning, hugged him too long and whispered, with the terrible clear sight of the ruined, *don't come back like me* — and then laughed it off and was gone up the lane before Cirilo could answer. Dolores took his face in both hands and looked at him a long while and gave him the language one last time, low and fast, a blessing older than the mission, and let him go. And Esteban stood a little apart, gray already though the day was young, his ruined hands working at the brim of his hat; and when his son came to him he could not, at first, find anything to say at all, because everything he wanted to say was *don't,* and he had decided in the night, watching Dolores press the collar, that he would not say it. So instead he said, roughly, "They will try to make you small up there. In a different way than here. A politer way." And then, against his whole life — against the culvert and the oak limb and forty years of being careful — he reached out and folded his son's pitching hand into a fist inside his own two stained ones, the old man's gesture, the gesture he had watched his own father make and had sworn in his fear never to make himself; and he held it a moment, and let it go, and turned and walked up the lane after his sons without looking back, because if he looked back his son would see his face.

Rebecca came to the platform itself, against every rule, to the very edge of it and no farther, because she could not afford to be seen, and they both knew it, and the knowing was part of the goodbye. She told him Santa Clara was only an hour, a morning's train — as though either of them believed for a single second that distance was the thing that kept them apart.

"It's a hundred years," he said, "and we both know it. Your people, my people —"

"Then go," she said, fierce and low, the tears standing in her eyes and refusing, by force of will, to fall where the platform could see them. "Go and become a man so loud they cannot pretend not to hear your name. And come back for me when you have."

She pressed something into his hand: the kid-leather glove, the soft one, the one she had worn the afternoon at the booth. "So you remember," she said, "whose hand you're playing for." The train called down the line. He closed his fist around the leather — her glove, made of the one material his family had ever truly owned, tanned God knew where by God knew whose stolen hands — and he stepped up into the car, and he did not look back, because he had learned that very morning from his father, without either of them ever saying it, that there are partings a man can only survive by refusing to witness them.

Through the window the platform slid away. Rebecca stood in the wrong place a moment too long — a beat past safety, a beat that was its own small act of defiance — and then made herself turn, and walk, alone, into a life that had just, in the space of one train whistle, quietly become a waiting. And the boy on the train rode north toward the colleges and the cathedrals and the sons of the men who had taken everything, with a white girl's glove shut in one fist and his abuelo's voice in one ear and his father's two warnings in the other, and a silence in him that had finally, after eighteen years, hardened all the way down into a purpose: he would climb, and he would not stop climbing, and he would make them say the name. Whatever it cost — and it would cost him, in the end, everything, though he could not yet know that, and would not have stepped off the train if he had — he would not, ever, for any price they named, agree to be second to the men who had decided he had been born it.

---

## _Siempre Segundo_ — Act II, novelized (full chapters)

### Chapter — The Chaparral

On the dry sun-facing slopes, where the redwoods could not hold and the meadows gave out, grew the chaparral — the tough gray-green scrub the comfortable never look at twice, the chamise and the ceanothus and the manzanita twisted like old arthritis — and it was the most stubborn and unkillable thing on the whole coast, the boy's people rendered into a single hardy plant.

It asked for nothing. It lived on the steep poor ground no gentler plant would fight for, in the long rainless heat, its little leathery leaves turned edge-on to the sun to spend no more water than it had to, its roots going down through cracked rock farther than the plant stood tall — a thing built entirely for endurance, for the long dry second-place seasons, for surviving on exactly what the better ground did not want. And it had a secret the redwoods did not have and did not need: it had made its peace with fire. The chaparral burns — it is built to burn, oily and ready — and where a redwood meets fire as a wound, the chaparral meets it as a door, because many of its seeds will not open, will not even consider living, until fire has passed over them; they lie in the dirt for years, for decades, waiting, and it is only the heat and the char and the smoke of the thing that destroys the parent that finally cracks them awake, so that the burned black slope nobody mourns is, by the next spring, greener and more alive and more crowded with new ceanothus than it had been in twenty unburned years.

There was the whole hard hope of his people in it, and Cirilo half-knew it as a boy and knew it completely at the end. That the ones assigned the poor steep dry ground learn to spend nothing and root deep and turn edge-on to the heat and endure the long seasons the favored never have to; and that the very fire that comes through and blackens everything and is mourned as catastrophe is also, for the ones built to wait in the dirt, the thing that finally cracks the future awake — that the burning and the renewal are not opposites but the same event, that a people can be charred to the black ground and be, in the seed, in the waiting unkilled seed, more ready to come up green than they had ever been; *we do not disappear,* said the chaparral, in the only way a plant can say anything, which is by being unkillable on the ground nobody else will have — burn us and we seed, cut us and we crown back from the root, and come the spring after the worst fire you can set, look at the black slope you wrote off, and count the green.

### Chapter — The Night Sky

Before the electric lights came, the dark on that coast was a real and total thing, and the sky over it was not the dim brown smudge later people would learn to settle for but the whole turning wheel of heaven laid open, the Milky Way thrown across it like a road of spilled salt, so many stars that the constellations drowned in them.

The boy lay out in it on summer nights, up the hill or out past the last house where the dark was cleanest, and felt the two things the great sky always gives at once and that only seem to contradict: how unspeakably small he was, a brown speck on a dark coast on a turning ball under a million suns that did not know his name and would not have been troubled to learn it — and, in the very same breath, how completely he belonged, made of the same fire as all of it, a piece of the burning thrown off and cooled into a boy for a little while and bound to go back. The coast people had read those stars for ten thousand years, had stories for them, had steered and planted and timed their lives by them; the stories were going out of the world now along with the language, but the stars were not, the stars were the one thing on that whole coast that no one could log or tan or fence or fell or legislate, the one wealth held perfectly in common, shining down with exact equality on the rancho and the tannery, on Whitfield's bluff and the dirt yard in the smoke, keeping no accounts.

He did not know, lying out under them at twelve, that the stars were the one piece of home that would make the whole journey with him. But they were. The same Orion that climbed over the bay in winter climbed over Boston too, and over the cold Brooklyn street where he would die — a little washed-out by the city's glare, but the same, the identical ancient fire — and on his last clear nights, when he could still get to a window, it was the sky he looked for, the one face of the west that the east could not deny him, the one thing that had answered *aquí estoy* across three thousand miles without his ever having to call. The covey could not reach him there. His mother could not. But the stars were over both of them at once, hers and his, the same wheel, and he took what comfort a dying man can take from the one thing in his life that had never once sorted him, and never would, and was shining, that very hour, with perfect indifference and perfect generosity, on a hill above a bay where someone would soon be digging.

### Chapter — The Threshold

He went up to the College of Santa Clara on the strength of one sentence from a man in a black coat, and he spent his first month there learning that a door opening is not at all the same thing as being welcome on the far side of it.

The college was Jesuit and old — older than the state, older than the conquest, the oldest school in California — and it was full, that autumn, of the sons of the men who had taken his family's land. Some of them he could even have named, had he wished to: a Bray, descended from the Boston captain who had danced the jota at the rancho and then filed on it; a boy whose grandfather had bought a Méndez quarter-league off the lawyer Quint for the price of a good horse. They were courteous to him in the particular way the secure are courteous to the useful — he was the arm that beat Stanford every spring, and so they let him sit at the edge of the long table, and did not, on the whole, let him forget that it was the edge. He wore his one good suit until it shone at the elbows. He learned which fountains and which staircases and which pews were, by an arithmetic no one had ever written down but every soul in the place could perform without error, *his.* He learned to construe Cicero and to serve at Mass and to hold his knife the way the others held theirs, and he learned all of it the way he had learned everything — in silence, watching, giving nothing back that could be used.

Father Fagan watched over him, in his fashion, which was a complicated fashion. The Jesuit was proud of him the way a man is proud of a fine tool he has found and sharpened, and fond of him the way a man grows fond of anything he has bet on; and there were nights, walking the cloister, when Fagan examined his conscience on the matter and did not love what he found there — that he had brought a brown boy into a house that would use him for nine innings every spring and never once truly seat him at its table, and that he, Fagan, was the man holding the door. He took the boy's confession on Saturdays. He never quite found the courage to make his own to the boy.

Cirilo did not mind the edge of the table, or told himself he did not, which at nineteen is very nearly the same thing. He had not come for the welcome. He had come because Rebecca Whitfield was an hour up the line at Stanford, close enough that on clear nights he fancied he could feel her there like heat off a wall; and because his abuelo had folded his fingers into a fist on a tannery floor and forbidden him to give the hands back; and because somewhere under the silence he had worn like a second suit his whole life, a thing had finally hardened all the way into a plan, cold and clear and patient as a winter creek. He would make himself so good that they would have no choice but to say his name. And then he would go and get her. He confessed the pride of it to Father Fagan twice, and was absolved twice, and kept it anyway — because some sins a man is not actually sorry for, and both he and the old priest knew it, and that shared knowledge was the nearest thing to friendship the house allowed them.

### Chapter — The Examination

There was a professor at Santa Clara, a layman named Pruett who taught rhetoric and resented having to teach it to the likes of Cirilo, and who found, in the oral examinations, his chance.

The orals were public — that was the cruelty built into them — conducted before the assembled class, a boy standing alone at the front construing Latin and defending propositions while his fellows watched and the professor probed. For most of the gentlemen's sons it was a formality, a genial hazing they had been bred for since the cradle, the Latin half-learned at good schools and the confidence wholly inherited. Pruett ran Cirilo's examination differently. He ran it long, and he ran it hard, doubling back, setting traps in the ablative, putting to the scholarship Mexican the questions he put to no one else, with a thin courteous smile that invited the room to enjoy what was coming — the bottle boy exposed at last as the fraud a man like Pruett needed him to be, because if the tannery's son could construe Cicero as well as the banker's son, then something Pruett had built his whole small life upon was not true, and Pruett would sooner break a boy in front of a class than let that be so.

Cirilo construed the Cicero. He had spent four hundred nights on it, in the silence, by a borrowed lamp, the way he had spent four hundred afternoons on the spitball — because he had understood from the first week that for him there would be no formality, no genial pass, no inherited confidence to coast upon; that he would be examined harder than any gentleman in the room and would therefore have to be *better,* simply to be permitted to be equal. And so he was better. He answered the traps. He found the verb. And when Pruett, furious now beneath the courtesy, abandoned rhetoric altogether for open baiting — asking the boy whether he did not think his talents might be better spent in *manual* work, at which his people had shown such aptitude — the room went still; and Cirilo, who had taken the slap on the pier and not swung, looked at the professor a long moment and answered, in Latin, quietly, with a line of the very Cicero they had been construing: the observation that it is the mark of a small mind to mistake the accident of birth for the measure of a man. Which was not, in fact, anywhere in the passage. Which Cirilo had composed on the spot, in correct Ciceronian Latin, and attributed to Cicero — gambling everything on the wager that Pruett, for all his airs, did not know the text half so well as the boy who had spent four hundred nights inside it. And Pruett, caught, uncertain, not daring to challenge a citation he could not place, went red, and nodded stiffly, and ended the examination; and the gentlemen's sons, who were not bad fellows and who loved a winner the way the young always do, broke into a startled applause the professor could not stop. Father Fagan heard of it within the hour. He laughed until he had to sit down — and then went to the chapel, troubled, and confessed that he had laughed, and admitted to God that he was not, in truth, entirely sorry, which was the same thing he was forever having to confess about that boy, and the same thing God, he suspected, was forever declining to mind.

### Chapter — An Hour Up the Line

Stanford was an hour up the line, and for three years Cirilo Méndez crossed that hour the way other men cross themselves — furtively, devoutly, and at the peril of his soul as the world around him reckoned souls.

They had worked out a geography of the forbidden. There was a grove of live oaks on the back edge of the Stanford lands, out past where the gentlemen rode, where a Santa Clara boy who came up on the milk train and walked the last two miles in the dark could meet a Stanford girl who had told her housemother she was at her devotions — and in a sense was. They had perhaps two hours, once a fortnight, when his games and her chaperones and the train schedule and the moon all conspired to allow it, and they spent those two hours talking, mostly — which is the thing no one believes about the young and the forbidden: that what they were starved for, even more than the other, was simply to be *known* by one other person in a world that had agreed to know them only as types.

She told him about her mother, the careless fever, the long conversion into an ornament. He told her — slowly, across many fortnights, because he had never told a living soul — about the rancho, and the courthouse, and his father's silence, and the boy named Toussaint his father had watched die from a culvert, which he had pieced together over the years out of things half-said and never finished. He told her things in that grove he would not tell her again until they had been married a decade, and some he never told her again at all. And Rebecca, who had been raised among people who used candor as a currency and conversation as a kind of fencing, discovered in the oak dark the disorienting experience of being told the truth by someone who wanted nothing from the telling except to set it down, once, in front of a person who would not use it. It was, she understood much later, the actual beginning of the marriage — not the wedding, years off, in the half-empty church, but those stolen fortnights in the trees, where two people each profoundly alone in their separate prisons taught each other the slow foreign language of not being.

They were nearly caught twice. Once a pair of riders passed close enough that Cirilo pressed her back against an oak with his hand over her own mouth and his heart going like a fist on a door, and they stood like that, breathing each other's breath, until the hooves faded — and afterward neither could speak, because they had both felt the same thing in the same instant, which was not fear exactly but the cold clear arithmetic of what discovery would cost. And it was not the same arithmetic for the two of them, and they both knew it. If they were caught, Rebecca faced disgrace, a ruined season, a swift correcting marriage to someone like Chuck. Cirilo faced a rope. They never once spoke of the difference. It sat between them in the dark like a third person, and it was, in the end, the truest measure both of what she was risking and of what he was — and of the fact that she climbed up to the grove anyway, every fortnight, and so did he.

### Chapter — The Steelhead

The steelhead lived the whole shape of Cirilo Méndez's life ten thousand times over before he was born to live it once.

It begins in the gravel of the upper creek, in the cold clean water under the redwoods, a fish no longer than a finger. And then the engine takes it — the same engine that drives the hawk and the spawning grass and every reaching thing on that coast — and it goes *down:* down the ravine the hawk flew down, down the San Lorenzo to the lagoon and out through the bar into the enormous salt, and there it grows, and silvers, and becomes a strong and beautiful thing in the open sea, ranging far, answerable to no one. It could stay. Nothing in the ocean compels it home. But the engine is patient and absolute, and one season it turns the silver fish around and points it back at the single creek out of all the creeks on that coast, the one it came from, and says: *up.*

And the fish climbs. Against the whole falling weight of the water it climbs — up the riffles, hurling itself at the falls again and again, battering its silver sides bloody on the rock, resting in the pools to gather what is left of itself and then climbing again, driven by a thing it did not choose and cannot refuse: to reach the exact gravel it was born in, and spawn, and make more of its kind. The current is always against it. That is the design. The climb is *into* the current, by definition, every inch of the way up — and most years most of them do not make it. The otter takes them, or the heron, or the drought leaves the creek too thin, or they simply spend the last of themselves on one fall a single leap too high and turn over, silver, in an eddy, and feed the same forest that made the fog that fed the creek they died climbing. The ones that fail are not less driven than the ones that arrive. They are only the ones the current happened to beat. Abel was a steelhead. Edward Purse was a steelhead — the strongest in the creek, turned back at a falls that men had built across the water for no reason but that they could. The river is full of them, the strong ones the current beat, and the river does not grieve them; and that is the river's mercy and the river's crime at once, and they are the same thing, as they always are.

But some make it. Some claw the whole of themselves up the river against the falling weight of the world and reach the high gravel and spawn — and here the steelhead tells the last of Cirilo's life, the part he could not yet know: that the climb spends you; that many a fish, having dragged its whole self up the river and given the gravel its only purpose, simply dies there, at the top, in the cold clean water it was born in, having arrived, having won, having made more of its kind — finished by the very reaching that completed it. The triumph and the dying are not two events. They are one event, seen from its two ends. The fish does not know it is heroic; it is only obeying the engine, which is another word for love, which is another word for the refusal to not-continue. And it climbs, into the current, and wins, and dies of the winning, and leaves the gravel thick with small finned things the length of a finger, that will go down to the sea in their turn and feel, one day, the same absolute summons to come home against the current and reach and spawn and be spent — *siempre,* the river says: second to nothing in this world but the engine itself, which made the river and the fish and the falls and the long doomed beautiful climb all at once, and called it good.

### Chapter — The Rematch

It came, the way these things do, on an ordinary afternoon the body remembers forever.

Stanford came down to Santa Clara in the spring, loud and cardinal-red and certain, and in the grandstand among the red sat a woman Cirilo had not seen in two years and had not stopped seeing for a single day. She had not expected to know anyone on the field. She was wrong.

At the plate, when his turn came, was Chuck Ashby, an S on his chest, tapping the corners as though he had bought them. "The bottle boy," he said down the line, and to his bench, loud enough for the red seats: "Three balls a nickel, fellas!" And the Stanford side laughed, the easy laugh of people who have never once had to wonder whether the laughter was about them.

Cirilo looked in. And there, half-risen from her seat in the sea of red, a kid-leather glove forgotten in her lap, was Rebecca. Their eyes met across two years. He set, and the first pitch was past Ashby before the laugh had finished. Strike one. The second buckled his knees, a wet ball that died in the air. Strike two, and the Stanford bench went quiet. He held the ball a moment — the stitched leather, an old friend — looked once more at the woman in the wrong-colored seats, and threw the best thing he owned.

Strike three. Ashby swung at air and nearly screwed himself into the dirt.

The Santa Clara side came up roaring, but Cirilo did not hear it, because Rebecca was on her feet — alone, in a sea of cardinal red, clapping for the wrong team and the wrong boy in front of every person she had been raised among, and not caring. *Costs a nickel,* he said to no one, under his breath. And up in the stands a woman who had everything to lose looked down at a man who had nothing, and chose, in public, out loud, and for good.

### Chapter — The Break

Her father gave her a choice the way men like her father give choices, which is to say he did not give her one at all.

It was a drawing room on Nob Hill, gaslight on good furniture, and Horace Whitfield stood at the mantel and addressed his daughter as though she were a board that had voted unwisely. A summer's foolishness, he said. A beach attendant. He had let it run because these things burn out. It had not burned out, and so now he would be plain: she would not see the Mexican again. Charley — Chuck, waiting by the window with the wounded patience of a man who believes he is owed something — had been a gentleman about all of it. More than she deserved.

"He throws a baseball, Rebecca," Chuck said softly, the knife slid in gently. "That's the whole of him. A strong arm and a borrowed suit."

"And what's the whole of you, Chuck?" she said, almost kindly. "Your father's name, your father's money, your father's chair at the club." A beat. "At least his arm is his."

Her father read her the terms like a sentence: walk out the door to the Mexican and she walked out of the family. No allowance, no name, no coming back when the romance of being poor wore thin — and it would wear thin, faster than she could dream. Rebecca looked around the warm, lit, comfortable cage of the room she had grown up in, and understood that she was being offered everything, and that everything was less than a man with one borrowed suit had already given her on a pier when he looked at her like a person and not an arrangement.

What she could not see, in her fury — and would understand only years later, when her own son was born into nothing — was that her father was not, in that moment, cruel so much as *terrified.* Horace Whitfield was not old money. He had come to San Francisco at seventeen with a dead father's debts and a cardboard valise, and clawed his way up through ship chandlery and railroad freight and a great deal he never afterward discussed, and the respectability he had bought with thirty years of that clawing was, to him, not a luxury but a seawall — the one thin thing standing between his family and the roaring cold water he had only just escaped. His daughter marrying a tannery hand, a Mexican, a *nobody,* was not breaking his heart; it was breaching the wall, and he could feel the water coming through it already. He was a frightened man bailing, and frightened men bailing will throw anything at all over the side, even a daughter, to keep the boat one more hour above the line. He never once put it to himself that way. He told himself, that night and for the rest of his life, that he was protecting her. The most dangerous men of that time and that place were, nearly every one of them, protecting someone.

She walked to the door, and turned back once, to Chuck: "Find someone who wants the chair at the club. They're easy to find. They're the only people you've ever met." And she went.

She paid for it in full and on time. The allowance stopped. The name closed over her like water. The friends she had grown up beside learned, all at once, not to see her on the street. And the heiress who had never made a bed or counted a dollar twice went to live in the margins of a country that had decided she no longer existed — and discovered, to the genuine astonishment of everyone who had warned her, that she had never once been happy until she did.

### Chapter — The Game

Underneath everything, it was always baseball. It had been baseball since the river stone and the tin can, since the overturned bucket behind the backstop, and it would be baseball at the grave; the game ran under all of it — the fog, the leather, the long dispossession — never once truly absent, because the game was the shape the whole of it took. And there is a reason the shape was baseball, and the reason is the truest thing there is to say about America.

Baseball is the only game built on failure. A great hitter fails seven times in ten and is called great for it. The season is a hundred and fifty games long and mostly made of losing; you cannot win it in a night or carry it on one afternoon's heroics; it rewards not the sprinter but the man who can fail all summer and keep his hands quiet and come back tomorrow with the same face. It is a pastoral game — a green field, open space, country distances, and no clock anywhere on it — set down in the middle of the most industrial, time-driven, whistle-blown century the world had yet built; and the country fell in love with it precisely because it was the thing the country felt itself losing: a green, slow, forgiving space where the clock did not run and a man got another at-bat tomorrow no matter how badly today had gone. The boy dreams it. The grown man grinds it. The old man, at the end, finally understands what it had been quietly telling him the whole time.
